BRADLEY. 206 REPORT OF THE PUTNEY LIBRARY SUB-COMMITTEE,. For the Year ended 31ST March, 1909. The Sub-Committee have pleasure in presenting the twenty-first annual report of the library. In December, 1908, Alderman Parker, Councillors Hoile, Jackson and Mathias were appointed by the Borough Council to act as the Sub-Committee for the year 1909. At the first meeting Alderman Parker was re-elected chairman for the year. The issue of the quarterly list of additions to the libiary hasbeen discontinued. A catalogue key to the indicator for classes F & J has been issued and has proved useful to many readers. The Sub-Committee take this opportunity of thanking all who* have furthered the usefulness and interest of the institution by the gifts of books or otherwise. The special thanks of the Sub Committee has been accorded to Mr. George Allen for his generous gift of an oil painting of old Putney Bridge; and to Sir W. J. Lancaster, J.P., for his gift of a water colour drawing of Putney Church. A list of donors will be found on page 211. The Finance Committee passed estimates of expenditure amounting to £1,265, and during the year this sum has been expended. Lending Department. During the year there has been an increase in the stock of this department of 642 volumes, 635 of which were added by purchase, and seven volumes by donation. The total number of volumes now available for issue is 10,816. Notwithstanding these additions to the stock, the Sub-Committee regrets to report that there is a slight decrease in the number of volumes issued for home reading. During the year 791 volumes have been rebound, 320 worn-out books have been replaced by new copies, while 71 obsolete books have been withdrawn from the stock. On March 31st, 2,145 boirowers' tickets, and 134 students' tickets were available for use in this department. 207 Reference Department. The books in this department are shelved in open cases, and readers have free access to the books, a privilege which is highly appreciated by the residents of Putney. The department is also used by many persons residing in the neighbouring districts. No records are kept of the books consulted, but it is estimated that upwards of 150 readers use the room daily. During the year 43 volumes have been added to the department, making the total stock at present 2,350 volumes. Magazine Rooms. These rooms continue to be used by a large number of readers, and it is very unusual to be able to find a chair vacant after 7 p.m. Thirty-eight monthly magazines and reviews, five quarterlies, and 49 weekly periodicals are placed in these rooms. The magazines and reviews are transferred to the lending department at the end of the month, where they are in great demand for home reading. News Room. The list of newspapers, &c., provided in this room includes 31 daily newspapers, among which are Birmingham, Cardiff, Dublin, Edinburgh, Liverpool, Manchester, and Plymouth daily journals, 56 weekly newspapers and periodicals and 52 monthly and quarterly publications. The Sub-Committee again have much pleasure 111 recording their satisfaction with the work of the Librarian and his staff. Tables of information relating to the issues of books, classification of stock, &c., will be found on pages 209 and 210. List of newspapers, periodicals, magazines, &c., will be found on pages 212 and 213. Signed on behalf of the Putney Library Sub-Committee. JAS. EDW. PARKER, May 8th, 1909. Librarian : THOMAS EVERATT. Librarians-in-charge : Balham Library : A. Cogswell. Tooting Library: E. Callard. o 216 REPORT OF THE SUB-COMMITTEE, 1908-9. In presenting their report for the year ended 31st March, 1909, the Sub-Committee have pleasure in stating that the Streatham, Balham, and Tooting Libraries continue to be extensively used in every department. The Tooting Public Library having become too small for the needs of the district Sir William J. Lancaster, J.P., L.C.C., most kindly supplemented his gift of five years ago by adding another storey to the building, and made such alterations and additions as were necessary for adapting the whole of the ground floor to Lending and Reference library purposes. The enlarged building, which is handsomely furnished throughout in oak, was formally handed over to the Borough Council on November 30th, 1908. It comprises—entrance hall, lending library, reference department, librarian's and staff rooms on the ground floor ; and a spacious well-lighted newsroom and two magazine rooms on the first floor. All the rooms are laid with oak block flooring, and are lighted throughout by means of electricity, the fittings being of special design. The new Lending Department is provided with bookcases toaccommodate 11,000 volumes, with room for further additions. In the planning, furnishing, and equipment of the building everything has been provided that would in any way conduce to the comfort of readers or promote the efficieut administration of the Institution. During the alterations and enlargement of the building the work of the library was carried on during four months at the Boys' School,. Church Lane. Books Issued. The number of books issued at the three libraries was. 416,333, an increase of 12,379 on last year's return. 217 This is the highest aggregate issue for one year since the establishment of the libraries :— The Tate Library, Streatham 183,675 Balham Public Library 156,004 * Tooting Public Library 76,6S4 Total 416,333 It is also gratifying to record the increasing use which is made of the more educative and technical sections of the libraries. • This is particularly noticeable at the Tate Library where the issue of books for home reading in the classes of literature other than Fiction and Juvenile Literature numbered 43,853 volumes. A General Subject-Catalogue of the books in the Balham Library was published in October. The Card Catalogues at each Library have been revised, and the usual supplementary and other lists issued at intervals during the year. Attention has also been given to the preparation of Subject-Catalogues for the Streatham and Tooting Libraries. Beading Booms. The iucreased accommodation provided at the Tooting Library, by the liberality of Sir William J. Lancaster, has relieved the crowding which frequently occurred there at busy times of the day. The rooms are supplied with a comprehensive selection of the leading newspapers and periodicals, and the large number of readers who constantly use the departments is an indication of their great popularity and usefulness. A list of the papers and magazines provided at each library is appended. See pages 224-229. Library Cash Beceipts. The following is a statement of the receipts from the sale of catalogues, fines, etc., at the three libraries:— £ s. d. The Tate Library, Streatham 142 14 7 Balham Public Library 82 3 1 Tooting Public Library 46 5 0 Total £271 2 8 Donors. A list of the Donors of books, etc., during the past year is given on page 223. The Sub-Committee desire specially to thank the Misses Lancaster, of Putney Hill, for their very kind gift of a graudfather's clock for the Tooting Public Library. The work of altering and completing the basement at the Balham Library was carried out -during the summer months. By these improvements ample room for storage and other purposes has now been provided at this library. 221 The increasing work at the Tate Library, combined with the difficulties arising from the present insufficient accommodation, will necessitate, at an early date, an extension of the building. The SubCommittee regret, however, that in consequence of the limitation of their funds, it will be impossible at present, without seriously curtailing the amount required annually for the purchase of books, to make such additions as are necessary for the growing requirements of the district, so as to increase still further the usefulness of this institution. The Sub-Committee are pleased to express their appreciation of the satisfactory and efficient manner in which the Librarian and his Staff have discharged their duties during the past year. Signed on behalf of the Sub-Committee, A. DOUGLAS ROBINSON, Chairman. 3rd May, 1909. 222 ANNUAL ISSUES AND STOCK Since the opening of the Libraries. Year. No. of Volumes in Stock. Librarian in-charge at Allfarthing Lane Branch Library: G. R. GATLAND. f 232 REPORT OF THE WANDSWORTH SUB-COMMITTEE For the Year ending 31st March, 1909. The Sub-Committee have again to report a most satisfactory increase in the issue of books, the number for the three libraries being 153,095, as compared with 137,762 in the year 1907-8—an increase of over 11 per cent, in the year and 82 per cent, since the Council took over the Library in 1901-2. It is especially gratifying to find that the Branch in Earlsfield, where from 1899 till 1904 no public library facilities existed, has increased its issue of books from 9,659 in 1907-8 to 15,193 in the past year—an increase of over 56 per cent. Eight books were stolen from the libraries by a man who had taken over 100 volumes from public libraries in or near London, and who was caught at Croydon and sentenced to six weeks imprisonment. Two sections of the Cotgreave Indicator, one for 4,000 and one for 2,000, have been purchased and placed—the former at West Hill and the latter at Allfarthing Lane. Vacant situations boards have been fixed outside the libraries at West Hill and Allfarthing Lane for the display of advertisements from the Daily Chronicle, Daily News, Daily Telegraph, and The Times, also for lists sent by the Wandsworth Employment Exchange. Boards have also been fixed, by consent of the Council, on their property in Magdalen Road and Pirbright Road. In consideration of exhibiting the advertisements of vacant situations, the proprietors of the Daily Telegraph present three copies of their paper daily, and those of The Times two copies daily. An agreement with the Metropolitan Water Board has been entered into in place of the old one with the late Southwark and Vauxhall Water Company. A clock has been purchased and fixed in the Earlsfield Branch Library. The only change in the staff has been caused by the resignation of R. Cruttenden, who has entered the service of the London County Council. A. Freeman has been appointed in his place. 233 The Sub-Committee desire to thank those who have kindly presented books, periodicals, &c., to the Libraries. A list of donors will be found below. The Sub-Committee are glad to state that the librarian and his assistants have ably and efficiently discharged their duties during the year. On behalf of the Sub-Committee, W. J. HARRISON, Chairman. Donations of books (one volume, except where the number is given) have been received from :—Association of Accountants; Australian Commonwealth ; Baptist Union ; Mr. E. Institutions 50 51 Zymotic Diseases, Death-rate from 50 52 Zymotic Diseases, Death-rate per 1,000 for Five Years and 1908 108 REPORT Of the fledical Officer of Health for the Year 1908. To the Mayor, Aldermen and Councillors of the Metropolitan Borough of Wandsworth. Sir and Gentlemen, I have the honour to present the Annual Report on the health and sanitary condition of the Borough for the year 1908. Population. The population of the Borough as estimated by the Registrar General was, in the middle of 1908, 289,506, an increase of 8,087 compared with 1907, but this estimate, in my opinion, is much under the actual population. The population on which all the rates in this Report are calculated has been obtained in a similar manner to that adopted in 1905, 1906, and 1907, by dividing the number of inhabitants at the Census, 1901, by the number of rated hereditaments in that year. The factor thus obtained represents the number of persons per rated hereditament, by which the number of hereditaments in the middle of 1908 is multiplied. b 6 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The population for the purposes of the Equalisation of Rates Act was estimated in 1906 as 296,020, in 1907, 308,663, and in 1908, 318,603, these populations being calculated from the number of rated houses. Table I. shows the population of each Ward and the number of rated hereditaments in 1901, the number of hereditaments in the middle of 1908 and the estimated population. TABLE I. Ward. The estimated uncorrected population for 1907 was 325,850, and in 1908 335,500, or an increase of 9,650, while the corrected population was, in 1907 320,120, and in 1908 329,700. The corrected population in Table II. is used to calculate the rates in this Report. The difference between the corrected and uncorrected population is made up by the population in the Public Institutions of /the Borough, which are as follows:— Clapham:—Hostel of God. Putney:—The Priory, Roehampton. Streatham:—St. Anne's Home. (St. Pancras parish.) Middlesex County Lunatic Asylum (part of). 8 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Tooting:—Tooting Home. Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals — The Fountain and the Grove. Newlands Asylum. Tooting Bee Asylum. Wandsworth:—The Workhouse. Royal Hospital for Incurables. Middlesex County Lunatic Asylum (part of). H.M. Prison. The total population of these Institutions was about 6,300, but as a proportion of the inmates of the Tooting Home, the Workhouse, and the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals belong to this Borough, a deduction of 5,800 made from the uncorrected total population gives, approximately, the corrected population, viz.:—329,700, compared with 320,120 in 1907, 310,310 in 1906, and 285,910 in 1905. Births. During the 53 weeks ending the 2nd January, 1909, 7,33r Dirths, 3,741 of males and 3,590 of females, were registered, compared with 7,036 in 1907, 7,057 in 1906, and 6,892 in 1905. The total number of births registered in this Borough was 7,321, but 80 births occurred in Institutions outside the Borough of persons belonging to the Borough, and 70 births in Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging to the Borough, making a corrected total of 7,331. In Clapham, Streatham and Wandsworth the rates were lower than in 1907, while the rate for the whole Borough was .17 per 1,000 lower, and was the lowest yet recorded. The lowest birth-rate was in Putney, where it was only 17.67, and the highest in Tooting, viz.:—33.65. 11 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Compared with the decennial average the birth-rate in all the sub-districts, with the exception of Tooting, and also in the whole Borough, was lower. In Clapham it was 4.18, in Putney 3.57, in Streatham 2.86, and in Wandsworth 4.33 lower, while in Tooting it was 2.10 higher. The birth-rate for the whole of London was 25.82 per 1,000, compared with 25.68 in 1907, 26.6 in 1906, 27.0 in 1905, and 28.14 the decennial average; while for England and Wales the rate was 26.5, compared with 26.3 in 1907, 27.0 in 1906, 27.2 in 1905, and 28.10 the decennial average. The rate of natural increase, or the excess of births over deaths was 11.98, compared with 11.30 in 1907, 11.59 in 1906, and 12.50 in 1905. Owing to the low death-rate during the year 1908, the rate of natural increase is higher than in the two previous years. Compared with the decennial average there has been a fall in the crude birth-rate of 12.2 per cent., compared with 12.27 per cent. in 1907, but as the changes in the age and sex population have been marked, it is therefore necessary to calculate the proportion of births to the number of possible mothers, or the total number of women living in the Borough at ages 15 to 45. In the year 1901 this rate was 88.6 per 1,000, while in 1907 it was only 74, and in 1908, 73; while for the whole of London the rate for 1906 was 97.5, and for 1907, 94.1. The legitimate birth-rate, calculated on the married female population was, in 1901, 215 per 1,000, and in 1908, 174; while for the whole of London it was, in 1907, 201.7, and for the five years 1902-06, 217.2. These rates are only approximately correct, and necessarily so, as all estimates of sections of the population become less reliable as the interval from the last Census increases. 12 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 155 births occurred in the Workhouse, nine of these belonging to Clapham, six to Putney, 23 to Streatham, 10 to Tooting, 38 to Wandsworth, 63 to Battersea, and six to other places. Of these 155 births 93 were illegitimate, and of these four belonged to Clapham, six to Putney, 17 to Streatham, six to Tooting, 15 to Wandsworth, 41 to Battersea, and four to other places. In the Borough, with the exception of the Workhouse, 129 illegitimate births were registered, 30 in Clapham, 13 in Putney, 23 in Streatham, 25 in Tooting, and 38 in Wandsworth. One illegitimate birth occurred in the Middlesex County Lunatic Asylum. The total number of illegitimate births was 177, 34 of which belonged to Clapham, 19 to Putney, 40 to Streatham, 31 to Tooting, and 53 to Wandsworth, compared with 189 in 1907, 203 in 1906, and 165 in 1905. The percentage of illegitimate births to total births was 2.41, compared with 2.68 in 1907, 2.87 in 1906, 2.4 in 1905, and 3.7 for the whole of London for 1907. The following Table shows the percentage of illegitimate births to total births for each sub-district and for the whole Borough for the year, and for the two previous years. 1906. 1907. 1908.[/### Clapham 3.0 2.7 2.45 Putney 1.6 2.8 3.42 Streatham 2.8 2.5 2.08 Tooting 3.7 3.2 2.88 Wandsworth 2.8 2.5 2.20 Whole Borough 2.87 2.68 2.41 13 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Deaths. During the 53 weeks ending the 2nd January, 1909, 3,230 deaths, 1,560 of males and 1,670 of females, were registered in the Borough, compared with 3,337 in 1907, 3,341 in 1906, and 3,241 in 1905. The total number of deaths is 107 less than in 1907, and 872 less than the decennial average corrected for increase of population. A still larger decrease in the number of deaths has occurred if the number of deaths be corrected for the additional week included in 1908. The total number of deaths after correction was 169 less than in 1907, and 934 less than the corrected decennial average. It is important to note that all the rates per 1,000 for the year 1908 should be reduced by the fraction 3/3 6/7 4/1 so as to compare more exactly with the rates in previous years. The uncorrected death-rate for the Borough was 9.63 per 1,000, compared with 10.24 in 1907, 10.57 in 1906, and 11.12 in 1905. The average for the decennium 1898-1907 was 12.21, or 2.58 above the rate for the year 1908. In the separate sub-districts this rate shows considerable variation, in Streatham the rate being only 7.17, while in Tooting it was 24.16. The reason for this high rate in Tooting is that in that sub-district there are a large number of Public Institutions, as the Fever Hospitals, Tooting Home, and Tooting Bee Asylum, where persons from other localities are received, and among whom the mortality is very high. 14 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. To ascertain the corrected death-rate corrections must be made to the above by the addition of the deaths in Out-lying Institutions of persons belonging to the Borough, and the subtraction of all deaths in Public Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, whose domiciles were in other parts of London. The total number of deaths for the 53 weeks, corrected for both internal and external Institutions was 3,380, compared with 3,416 in 1907, 3,461 in 1906, 3,320 in 1905, 3,417 in 1904, 3,023 in 1903, 3,328 in 1902, 3,130 in 1901, and 4,209, the decennial average corrected for increase of population. The number of corrected deaths for the 53 weeks of the past year was less than the number of deaths in the 52 weeks of 1907. During the year 731 deaths occurred in Public Institutions and 87 in other places not institutions in the Borough. Of these 818,633 deaths were of persons not belonging and 185 of persons belonging to the Borough. Of the deaths in other places not Public Institutions 15 occurred in Clapham, 19 in Putney, 28 in Streatham, nine in Tooting, and 16 in Wandsworth. Table VI. shows the deaths in Public Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, arranged as to ages, causes of death, and Institution in which the death occurred. The greatest number of deaths was due to Old Age, and of the 156 registered, 109 occurred in Tooting Bee Asylum, and 21 in the Tooting Home. In the Middlesex County Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Old Age, diseases of the Respiratory Organs, Bright's disease, and all other causes, which include all diseases of the nervous system. In Tooting Bee Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, diseases of the Respiratory Organs, Old Age, Bright's disease, Cancer, and all other causes; in the Hostel of God from Tubercular disease of the Lungs and Cancer; and in the Tooting Home from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Old Age, and diseases of the Respiratory Organs. In the Fever Hospitals, one death occurred from Measles, 46 from Scarlet Fever, compared with 49 in 1907, 27 from Diphtheria, compared with 45, one from other forms of Tuberculosis, one from Developmental diseases, and two from all other causes. 17 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In the next Table the number of deaths in Institutions outside the Borough is given in detail, but a complete list of these Institutions with the number of deaths that occurred in each during the year, is given after Table I. of the Local Government Board's Tables. As the Borough is dealt with as one Sanitary District the deaths of persons belonging to other parishes in the Borough in Institutions such as the Fever Hospitals, Tooting, are considered to be deaths in Internal Institutions with regard to the whole Borough, and are not included in the following Tables. The same applies to deaths in the Hostel of God, Clapham, the Tooting Home, the Workhouse, the Tooting Bee Asylum, and the Royal Hospital for Incurables. The three deaths in the Prison, all of which were due to Tubercular diseases, were of persons described in the Registrar's returns as homeless, but in all probability they did not actually belong to this Borough. 21 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of deaths in out-lying Institutions was 783, 432 of males and 351 of females, compared with 739 in 1907, 731 in 1906, and 689 in 1905. 326 of these deaths occurred in the Union Infirmary, compared with 376 in 1907, 433 in General and Special Hospitals, 68 of which occurred in Public Lunatic or Imbecile Asylums, compared with 357 in 1907, and 14 in the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals, compared with six in 1907. Of the 433 deaths in General and Special Hospitals, 69 occurred in St. Thomas's Hospital, 43 in Bolingbroke Hospital, 24 in Belgrave Hospital, 18 in Victoria Hospital, 18 in St. George's Hospital, 15 in Westminster Hospital, 15 in Guy's Hospital, 15 in Horton Asylum, 12 in Banstead Asylum, 12 in Charing- Cross Hospital, and nine in Cane Hill Asylum. The number of deaths in these and other Institutions outside the Borough is shown on Table XII. The corrected number of deaths after adding the deaths of persons belonging to the Borough and subtracting those of persons dying in the Borough and not belonging thereto, was 3,380, 1,669 °f males and 1,711 of females, a decrease of 36 compared with 1907, and a decrease of 829 compared with the corrected •decennial average. The corrected death-rate for the year was 10.25, compared with 10.67 in 1907, 11.15 in 1906, 11.6i in 1905, 12.93 in 1904, 12.24 in 1903, 13.43 in 1902, and 15.33 in 1901. Corrected for the additional week included in the year 1908, the number of deaths was 3,315, and the death-rate 10.05 Per 1,000. Table IX. shows the total number of deaths and the deathrates for each sub-district and for the whole Borough, corrected and uncorrected for deaths in Public Institutions. The death-rate for the whole of London for the year was 14.75 Per 1,000, compared with 14.55 in 1907, 15,07 in 1906, 15,1 in 1905, i6.10 in 1904, 15.6 in 1903, and 17.2 in 1902. 23 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The death-rate in England and Wales in 1908 was 14.7 per 1,000, compared with 15.0 in 1907, 15.4 in 1906, 16.2 in 1905, and 16.2 in 1904; in the 76 Great Towns 14.9, compared with 15.4; and in the 142 Smaller Towns 14.o, compared with 14.5. If a comparison is to be made with other places it is necessary to again correct the corrected rate, viz. :—10.25, for age and sex distribution. This is done by multiplying the rate by a factor obtained by dividing the standard death-rate in England and Wales, at the Census of 1901, with the standard death-rate for the Borough at the same period. This factor has been ascertained to be 1.0547, which gives a rate for this Borough for the year 1908 of 10.81 per 1,000, compared with 11.25 in 1907, 11.76 in 1906, 12.24 in 1905, 13.6o in 1904, 12.87 in 1903, and 14.00 in 1902. In the separate sub-districts of the Borough the corrected death-rate was lowest in Streatham, viz. :—9.01, and highest in Tooting, viz. :—12.14. With the exception of Clapham the rate for the 53 weeks of 1908 was lower in all the sub-districts than the rate for the 52 weeks of 1907. The greatest reduction occurred in Tooting, where the rate for 1907 was 13.25 and in 1908 12.14 per 1,000. The rates in this Table do not agree with the rates in Table XI., as the rates in the latter Table are calculated on the total population without deducting the population of non-parishioners in Public Institutions in the Borough. The first Chart shows the death-rate in the Borough for each fortnight compared with the average for the quinquennium 19031907. This Chart shows that only on three occasions was the rate higher, viz. :—the first and second fortnights of February, and the first fortnight of March. In May, June, July, August, September and October the fortnightly rates were much under the quinquennial average, in the first fortnight of August the rate having been only 6.80 per 1,000, compared with 10.52, the quinquennial' average. On only one occasion, viz. :—in the first fortnight of February was the rate above 14 per 1,000, while on 15 occasions the rate was under 10 per 1,000. The next Chart shows the annual death-rate per 1,000 during the 53 weeks of the year for the whole of London and for each fortnight for the whole Borough. During the whole year the rate was under the rate for the whole of London, and this was specially the case in the first five months of the year, and also in the months of October and November. Under 1 year. 1 to 5 years. 5 to 15 years. 15 to 25 years. 25 to 65 years. 65 years and upwards Clapham 18.81 7.88 2.90 2.77 37.49 30.15 Putney 17.48 5.18 2.59 3.23 34.95 36.57 Streatham 16.79 7.37 1.19 6.07 37.16 31.42 Tooting 34.47 13.95 3.68 3.42 25.79 18.69 Wandsworth 23.35 8.81 3.63 3.06 35.02 26.13 Whole Borough 21.31 8.46 2.72 3.87 35.09 28.55 Compared with 1907 the percentage of deaths under one year was .38 higher, due to the slight increase in the number of deaths of infants from Diarrhoea and Zymotic Enteritis; from one to five years .35 lower; from five to 15 years 1.11 lower; from 15 to 25 years .12 higher; from 25 to 65 years 2.80 higher; and from 65 years and upwards 1.84 lower. There was a considerable diversity in these rates in the different sub-districts. In Streatham the rate under one year was only 16.79, while in Tooting it was 34.47, while over 65 years of age Tooting has a rate of 18.69 and Putney of 36.57. Again, at from one to five years the rate was still high in Tooting and low in Putney, and also at ages five to 15 and 15 to 25 years, while at ages 25 to 65 and over 65 the Tooting rate was much lower and was the lowest of the five sub-districts. 34 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. As stated in previous reports, these differences are due to the difference in age distribution and social conditions in the subdistricts. Infantile Mortality. The total number of deaths of infants under one year was 720, compared with 715 in 1907, 874 in 1906, 818 in 1905, 851 in 1904, and 743 in 1903. Of these, 136 occurred in Clapham, 54 in Putney, 155 in Streatham, 131 in Tooting, and 244 in Wandsworth. The infantile mortality, or deaths under one year to 1,000 births was 98, compared with 101 in 1907, 125 in 1906, 118 in 1905, 123 in 1904, and 110 in 1903. This rate was 98 in Clapham, compared with 90 in 1907 and 124 the decennial average; 97 in Putney, compared with 109 and 125; 80 in Streatham, compared with 88 and 112; 122 in Tooting, compared with 148 and 152; and 101 in Wandsworth, compared with 97 and 133. In the whole Borough the rate was 3 per 1,000 births under that for 1907, and 28 under the decennial average. The rate in 1908 was much lower than any rate ever recorded, and in each sub-district it shows a marked diminution as compared with the decennial average, in Putney showing a difference of 38, and in Wandsworth of 32 per 1,000, compared with that average. The following Table shows the number of deaths, the percentage to total deaths, the rate per 1,000 births for the year and for the three previous years for each sub-district and for the whole Borough. The Tables which follow are inserted at the request of the Local Government Board and show in detail the deaths of infants under one year, arranged in monthly age periods and from certain diseases. Table XIX. shows the deaths in the whole Borough and the six following Tables the deaths in each registration sub-district. The total number of births was 7,331, of which 7,154 were legitimate and 177 illegitimate, and the total deaths 720, 653 legitimate and 67 illegitimate. There were five deaths from the notifiable diseases, one from Scarlet Fever and four from Diphtheria. One of the deaths from Diphtheria occurred in Clapham, one in Tooting, and two from Diphtheria and one from Scarlet Fever in the Springfield sub-district. 14 deaths occurred from Measles, 25 from Whooping Cough, 148 from Diarrhoeal diseases, 290 from Wasting diseases, 26 from Tubercular diseases, 134 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, eight from Meningitis, seven from Syphilis, and 68 from other causes. Compared with 1907, there has been an increase in the number of deaths from the notifiable diseases, from Diarrhoeal diseases, Wasting diseases, and Tubercular diseases, and a decrease in the number of deaths from Measles, Whooping Cough, diseases of the Respiratory Organs, Meningitis, and other causes. In all the deaths, with three exceptions, two in Putney and one in Streatham, the cause of death was certified by a medical man or by a coroner after inquest. 32'6 per cent. of the deaths occurred in the first month, compared with 33.7 in 1907 and 32.03 in 1906; 55" 1 per cent. in the first three months, compared with 53 and 51.7; and 77.7 in the first six months, compared with 73 and 72.6. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 37 The Table also shows that 290, or 40,3 per cent, of the deaths occurred from Wasting diseases, and of these 181, or 62.4 per cent. occurred under one month. Of the total deaths under one month, viz. 235, 181 or 77 per cent. occurred from Wasting diseases, compared with 74 per cent. in 1907. From Diarrhoeal diseases only 13 deaths occurred under one month. The greatest mortality from these diseases occurred at two to three months. From Tubercular diseases 26 deaths occurred, compared with 19 in 1907, 42 in 1906, and 34 in 1905 ; and from diseases of the Respiratory Organs 134, compared with 158, 112 and 136. Eight deaths were registered as due to Meningitis, but enquiries of the medical attendant showed that these were due to Posterior-basic Meningitis with two exceptions, both occurring in hospital, where the deaths were registered as having occurred from Cerebro-spinal Meningitis. The preceding Table shows approximately the proportion of deaths per 1,000 births for each week under one month, and for each month to one year of age, from four groups of diseases. Compared with 1907 the rate from common infectious diseases is lower, this having been due to the decrease in the number of deaths from Measles and Whooping Cough. From Diarrhœal diseases the rate is higher, from Wasting diseases slightly lower, from Tubercular diseases higher, and from other causes lower. Compared with the rate for England and Wales for 1907, the proportion of deaths under one year from infectious diseases is lower, having been 6.16 in Wandsworth, compared with 8.67. The proportion of deaths from Diarrhœal diseases was 20.72 in Wandsworth, and in England and Wales 14.06. From Wasting diseases the rate in this Borough was 38.92,. compared with 43.18 in England and Wales for 1907. From Tubercular diseases the rate in this Borough was 3.64, compared with 2.75 in 1907, and with 4.54 in England and Wales for 1907. Compared with the rate in England and Wales for the five years 1902-1906 the rate in this Borough was, from infectious diseases 227 lower, from Diarrhœal diseases 4.37 lower, from Wasting diseases 6.07 lower, from Tubercular diseases .90 lower, and from other causes 18.60 lower. The rate for 1908 thus compares most favourably with the rate for England and Wales for the quinquennium 1902-1906. It will also be seen from the preceding Table that the rate from common infectious diseases was highest at 10-11 months, from Diarrhœal diseases at two to three months and five to six months, from Wasting diseases under one week, from Tubercular diseases at 9-10 months, and from other causes at one to two and two to three months. From all causes the rate was highest under one week and lowest at 11-12 months. 47 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. It will here be seen that of the total number of cases remaining on the books as unvaccinated, viz.:—316, no less than 223 were in the Clapham sub-district. It is difficult to account for the enormous difference between the sub-districts except on the grounds that the administration of the Vaccination Act in Clapham is defective. If Small-pox once gets a footing in this sub-district, its spread will be rapid and its results disastrous. Panic vaccination is expensive and not always efficient, and I am of opinion that this matter merits the serious consideration of the Borough Council. Scarlet Fever. 1,232 cases of Scarlet Fever were notified during the year,, compared with 1,330 in 1907, 1,069 in and 1,494 in 1905. 232 of these were in Clapham, 66 in Putney, 270 in Streatham, 218 in Tooting, and 446 in Wandsworth. 1,072 or 87 per cent. of these cases were removed to hospital' where 19 died, and 160 or 13 per cent. were treated at home with two deaths. The total number of deaths was 21, of which four were in Clapham, two in Putney, five in Streatham, one in Tooting, and nine in Wandsworth. The percentage of cases removed to hospital, viz.:—87, is the highest recorded, as in 1907, 84.8 per cent, were removed; in 1906, 83.8; in 1905, 84.1; in 1904, 81.6; in 1903, 78.1; in 1902, 75; and in 1901, 70.6 per cent. The case mortality was 1.7 per cent. compared with 1.65 in 1907, 1.78 in 1906, 1.87 in 1905, and 1.74 in 1904. The mortality among cases removed to hospital was 1.77 per cent., compared with 1.77 in 1907, and among those treated at home 1.25, compared with .99 in 1907. The mortality among the home treated cases is much lower than among those cases treated' in hospital, while in 1907 the rates were respectively 1.77 and .99. 61 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The proportion of cases notified per 1,000 of population was 3.67, compared with 4.08 in 1907, 3.4 in 1906, 5.1 in 1905, and 2.8 in 1904, and in the County of London 4.60, compared with 5.53, 4.3, 4.1 and 2.89. The proportion of cases notified was thus .93 per 1,000 lower in this Borough than for the whole of London, while in 1907 it was 1.45 per 1,000 lower. Scarlet Fever and Schools. The next Table shows the number of cases of Scarlet Fever for each month of the year among children in attendance at elementary and other schools for each sub-district, also the number for the whole Borough, the number of cases among persons not at school, the number attending schools outside the Borough, as well as the number of cases in which, as far as could be ascertained, the diagnosis of Scarlet Fever was incorrect. Of the total number of cases, viz.:—1,232, the diagnosis was incorrect in 44 or 3.5 per cent.; 632 or 51.3 per cent. were in attendance at schools in the Borough; 71 or 5.8 per cent. attended schools outside the Borough; while 485 or 39.4 per cent. did not attend school, being either above or under school age. The percentage of children attending school varied somewhat in the sub-districts. For example: in Clapham 109 out of 232, or 64 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 47 per cent. were at school; in Putney 25 out of 66, or 38 per cent.; in Streatham 144 out of 270, or 53.3 per cent.; in Tooting 94 out of 218, or 43.1 per cent.; and in Wandsworth 260 out of 446, or 56.1 per cent. The percentage in Tooting is lower for two reasons than it is in reality, one being that a number of children in Tooting attend schools in Balham, and the other that 21 cases were notified from the Fever Hospitals and the Tooting Bec Asylum. The two schools chiefly affected in Clapham were Old Town and St. Andrew's Street, 18 cases having occurred among scholarsattending the former and 26 the latter. 19 of the cases in St. Andrew's Street school occurred in September, October and November, but on visiting the school I was satisfied that everything possible was being done to prevent the spread of the disease. The names and addresses of all absentees were noted and their homes visited and, as a result of the measures taken, no cases occurred in December. In Streatham, Broadwater Road and Smallwood Road schools were chiefly affected, and in both the cases chiefly occurred in the last four months of the year. In Tooting, Ensham Street, Tooting Graveney, and Sellin court Road schools had the largest number of cases, and in the first-mentioned 12 cases occurred in the month of November These were due chiefly to unrecognised cases. In Wandsworth, Scarlet Fever was most prevalent at Swaffield Road school, 49 cases having been notified during the year. These cases occurred in every month, but the greatest number was notified in October. The reason for this increase was that of 13 cases notified in that month, six were in one house, and 10 in the same street. The total number of cases notified was 581 (91 in Clapham, 39 in Putney, 137 in Streatham, 89 in Tooting, and 225 in Wandsworth), compared with 629 in 1907, and 566 in 1906. This number, although 46 above the uncorrected, is 114 under the corrected decennial average In Clapham there has been an increase of 12, in Putney a decrease of 26, in Streatham a decrease of 20, in Tooting a decrease of 49, and in Wandsworth an increase of 35, compared with 1907. The greatest number of cases occurred in Wandsworth and the smallest in Putney. The number of cases notified per 1,000 of population was 1.73, compared with 1.93 in 1907, and 1.79 in 1906, and in London 1.64, compared with 1.65 and 1.70. 469 or 80.7 per cent. of the cases were removed to hospital where 33 died, and 112 or 19.3 per cent. were treated at home with four deaths. The case mortality was 6.3 per cent., compared with 6.3 in 1907, 8.9 in 1906, 8.9 in 1905, and 10.8 in 1904. The case mortality for the whole of London for the year 1908 was 9.2 per cent., compared with 8.89 in 1907, 8.7 in 1906, and 8.5 in 1905. The case mortality among cases treated in hospital was 7.03 per cent., and among those treated at home 3.6 per cent. The great reduction in the mortality is to a large extent due to the earlier use of antitoxin, both in the cases treated in hospital and in those treated at home. During the year, however, a considerable proportion of the cases admitted to hospital have been of a more virulent type, and consequently the mortality is slightly increased. CHART Showing the number of cases of DIPHTHERIA notified during each week of the year 1908. 71 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Chart shows the number of cases notified during each week of the year. The maximum number of cases, viz.:—21, was notified in the second week of April, and the minimum, viz.:—two, in the fifth week of August. The period of school holidays in July and August was particularly free from the disease. In September and October the number of cases slightly increased, and another maximum was reached in the second week of November, when 15 cases were notified. At no period of the year was there any definite outbreak, and this is doubtless due to the methods adopted, not only in this Borough, but in the Metropolis generally for dealing with cases of Diphtheria in schools. The Medical Officer to the Education Department, London County Council, receives copies of all notifications of infectious disease, and when his attention is called to a succession of cases in one school means are at once taken to swab the throats of all children in the classrooms in which cases have occurred. Meanwhile, the officers of the Sanitary Authority have obtained lists of absentees from schools, and all the homes are visited, and if any cases of sore throat are found the throats of these children are at once swabbed and a bacteriological examination made. In this way any outbreak is prevented, and no child who has had sore throat is permitted to return to school until a negative bacteriological result has been obtained. This method has been carried out during 1908 most successfully, and I am certain that it was the means of preventing serious and fatal outbreaks in several schools in the Borough. Of the remaining cases, viz.:—338, in 54 or 9.3 per cent., the diagnosis was incorrect, and 294 or 50.6 per cent. were not in attendance at school. 74 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. From these figures it would appear that school infection was not so important a factor in the spread of Diphtheria as in 1907, when the percentage of children in attendance at school was 49, and not in attendance 44. A further examination of the Table shows that in Putney four cases only occurred in connection with schools in the last nine months of the year, and if it had not been for the eight cases which occurred in Roehampton in January, only 11 cases would have been notified from that sub-district. Of the total cases notified, 54 were incorrectly diagnosed, compared with 44 in 1907, leaving a total of 527, compared with585 in 1907, 531 in 1906, and 312 in 1905. Only 11 cases occurred in connection with private schools, compared with 21 in 1907, and 48 in 1906. In all, 21 schools remained free from Diphtheria, compared with 17 in 1907, and of these 17 were non-provided schools. In one school, viz.:—Immanuel School, Streatham, swabs were taken by the Assistant Medical Officer, London County Council, during the year, and as a result of this three "carrier" cases were discovered in Standard I., in which standard all the notified cases occurred. These cases were kept under observation and not permitted to return to school until a bacteriological examination had been made and a negative result obtained. Swabs were also taken at Roehampton School on several occasions during the year for the purpose of ascertaining if possible how long the Klebs Loeffler bacillus continues in the throats of school children, and also how long the virulence of the bacillus lasts, but this investigation is still going on, and so far no definite report can be made. With regard to the 60 cases notified from the Union Infirmary, these all occurred during the first four months of the year. Of these, 48 were stated to have come from the Workhouse, one from the Intermediate Schools, and 11 from houses in this Borough. With few exceptions, all these were cases of bacteriological Diphtheria, that is, they were only notified as Diphtheria after swabs had been taken. All the 60 cases were removed to hospital, and further bacteriological examinations at the hospital showed that in 21 of the cases the Klebs Loeffler bacillus was found, but in only six was the bacillus virulent. In 39 of the cases examined at the hospital the Klebs Loeffler bacillus was absent. 76 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the six positive cases, five had been in the Infirmary over two months, while one was admitted seven days prior to notification. This case was sent to the Infirmary from the Workhouse on account of Bronchitis, and no symptoms of Diphtheria were at that time observed by the Medical Officer of that Institution. No deaths from Diphtheria occurred among- these 60 cases, but one child notified died from Whooping Cough, from which disease it was suffering when removed. Of the total number of cases of Diphtheria, 22 or 3.7 per cent, occurred among children under one year of age, 210 or 36.1 per cent, occurred among children from one to five years, 262 or 45.0 per cent, from five to 15 years, and 87 or 14.9 per cent, over 15 years. Of the 469 cases removed to hospital, 54 were reported not to be suffering from Diphtheria. Drainage defects were found in 56 of the houses in which cases of Diphtheria occurred, compared with 92 in 1907. Inquiry into the return cases of Diphtheria revealed some facts which were, from an administrative point of view, of importance. Cases 4, 5, 6, and 7, were all members of the same family and were presumably infected by Case 5, which had been removed to hospital on March 20th, suffering from Scarlet Fever, and discharged on May 14th. The mother of this child, No. 4, was taken ill on May 16th, with Diphtheria, followed by definite clinical cases of Diphtheria in the case of other two members of the same family, the first symptoms in these latter appearing about the 23rd. The return case was also removed to hospital on the 21st, after a swab had been taken from the throat, in which a positive result was obtained, showing that this case was a definite one of Post Scarlatinal Diphtheria. Cases 10 and xi were also cases of Post Scarlatinal Diphtheria, No. 10 having been 11 days at home before definite symptoms appeared, but No. 11 was ill when it returned home. Nos. 12 to 17 were all members of the same family, and the diagnosis was verified in all these cases by bacteriological examination. No. 15 was the case removed on February 12th, and returned on March 26th. As it was suspected that this case was the cause of the other eases, a swab was taken from his throat, and a positive result was obtained. None of these six cases proved fatal. Enteric Fever. 60 cases of Enteric Fever were notified during the year (14 from Clapham, five from Putney, 12 from Streatham, nine from Tooting, and 20 from Wandsworth), compared with 70 in 1907, 96 in 1906, and 96 in 1905. 43 of the cases were removed to hospital where four died, and 17 were treated at home with three deaths. 82 Report ot the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of deaths was seven (three in Clapham, one in Putney, two in Streatham, none in Tooting, and one in Wandsworth), compared with 10 in 1907, 15 in 1906, 11 in 1905, and 11 in 1904. The number of deaths was three less than in 1907, and 15*1 under the decennial average. The proportion of cases notified per 1,000 of population was 18, compared with .21 in 1907, .30 in 1906, and .33 in 1905, and with .28 for the whole of London. The corrected death-rate from Fever was '02 per 1,000, compared with .03 in 1907, .048 in 1906, .04 in 1905, and .04 in 1904, while the rate for the whole of London was '04, compared with .04 in 1907, .05 in 1906, .05 in 1905, and .06 in 1904. Of the 43 cases removed to hospital, 13 or 30 per cent, were found not to be suffering from Enteric Fever, compared with 23.3 per cent, in 1907. The Chart shows the number of cases notified during the year. In 20 of the weeks during the year no cases were notified, one -case each in 13 of the weeks, two cases each in 15 of the weeks, three cases each in three of the weeks, and four cases each in two of the weeks. Of the 60 cases notified, 14 were not Enteric Fever, 13 being hospital cases, and one treated at home, while one was notified from the Prison, leaving 45 cases in which special inquiries were made as to the probable cause of the disease. In 11 of the cases the disease was in all probability contracted by eating shell-fish, in eight it was contracted outside the Borough (one in Ireland, one in Manchester, one in Devonport, one abroad, one in Ramsgate, and three in other places), in two the infection was contracted from other cases, in one probably from watercress, while in 24 no history of infection could be ascertained. With regard to the 11 cases caused by shell-fish, three were from oysters, five from mussels, and three from winkles. Cases of Mistaken Diagnosis. In no cases (44 of Scarlet Fever, 54 of Diphtheria, and 12 of Enteric Fever), information was received from the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals that the patients were not suffering from the disease notified or any other notifiable disease, compared with 111 in 1907, 93 in 1906, 97 in 1905, and 94 in 1904. Of the 1,584 cases removed to hospital, in no or 6.9 per cent, the diagnosis was incorrect, compared with 6.5 per cent, in 1907, 6.7 in 1906, 5.2 in 1905, and 10 in 1904. Cerebro-Spinal Fever. Five cases of Cerebro-spinal Fever were notified during the year (one from Clapham, two from Streatham, one from Tooting, and one from Wandsworth), compared with three in 1907. One of the cases was notified from St. Thomas's Hospital, one from the Belgrave Hospital for Children, and one from the Westminster Hospital. These cases proved fatal. The diagnosis in these cases was confirmed by lumbar puncture and bacteriological examination. In the other two cases, which recovered, no bacteriological examination was made. In London 85 cases of this disease were notified during the year, compared with 153 in 1907. 17 deaths were registered as having been caused by Meningitis (four in Clapham, two in Putney, six in Streatham, and five in Wandsworth). Six of these were of infants under one year, five from one to five years, and six over five years of age. As in previous years, inquiries were made from the medical attendant, and in all the cases the disease was stated to have been simple Meningitis. Bacteriological Examinations. Although the mortality from Diarrhoea and Zymotic Enteritis was greater than in 1907, yet it was much under the average, and without taking the increase of population into consideration, the total number of deaths was less in only three years of the decennium, viz. :—1902, 1903, and 1907. In these years, as in 1908, climatic conditions were not favourable to the disease. In only 31 days of the 92 in the third quarter was the temperature above the average, and these days were confined to the first and last weeks of July, the first week in August, and the last 10 days of September. Another important factor which has, in my opinion, had a beneficial effect, was the work done by the female Sanitary Inspectors, and although this cannot be put in statistical form, yet, from the reports made to me fortnightly by these Inspectors, it is evident that their labours have been largely helpful in preventing the occurrence of these diseases. In Tooting, which had the highest Diarrhoea mortality, a considerable amount of supervision is exercised by these Inspectors, and in that sub-district the mortality has diminished most markedly; the number of deaths having been reduced from 28 to 18, and the death-rate from .92 to .57. During the year the female Sanitary Inspectors have continued the work begun in 1905, in visiting homes where births occurred, and the following report gives the results of such visits and a general account of the work carried out:— "We have the honour of submitting to you a report of our visits amongst infants during the year 1908. "It is gratifying to be able to state that we have in a measure widened the scope of our work, having visited infants in Putney and Southfield in addition to Tooting, the Fairfield and Springfield Wards of Wandsworth, and the poorer parts of Balharn, to which our work was limited in former years. More second visits, too, have been paid in Tooting and Wandsworth. 100 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "We have found, during the year, many mothers anxiously awaiting us, whom we had visited previously with other children; the pamphlets were carefully kept, many things we had told them remembered, and the older children shown with much pride as having been brought up according to our advice. "In only one instance was the pamphlet refused, and in that case the mother was a foreigner who did not seem to understand the trend of the conversation. Several fathers have expressed their gratitude to us for our visits, and have desired their wives to follow carefully our instructions, in contrast to one or two men in former years who forbad their wives to admit us or to take the pamphlet. One or two midwives have asked for copies also. "The total number of visits paid after notification of births will be found in the first of our Tables, No. XLIX., which also shows the method of feeding at the first visit, and the number of infants visited who died during the year. Although the number of homes visited where deaths from Diarrhoea and Enteritis had occurred was far greater this year than last (115 as against 76 in 1907), only 20 had been previously visited by us (Balham three, Tooting six, Wandsworth eleven). Of the 20, five were delicate from birth, two were twins, and one was the eldest of triplets, three were entirely breast-fed, two of whom died in October from bronchitis and pneumonia in addition to Enteritis, and the other was delicate from birth. "Tooting is not the 'black spot' it was last year, the proportion of deaths being considerably lower. "Of the 1,996 infants visited during the year, 46 died in addition to the 20 previously mentioned, who succumbed to Diarrhoea and Enteritis. Of the 46, 4 died from Prematurity —2 being twins; this may seem a small proportion, but the majority of deaths from this cause occurred within a few days of birth. 13 died from Bronchitis and Pneumonia, which have been exceedingly prevalent this winter, and indeed, on some days in nearly every house we visited, the baby was Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 101 found to be suffering from one or other of these complaints. Only one death, however, occurred from Whooping Cough. 10 were registered as having died from Marasmus and Atrophy. Eight died from Tuberculous diseases, one being from Phthisis. The other deaths were from various causes, chiefly Dentition and Convulsions. "There is a decrease in the number of ineffectual visits as compared with last year, this being due to the fact that, instead of making five or six attempts to see the mother, as before, this year, after two or three unsuccessful visits, we wasted no further time and thus were enabled to visit more infants. At the same time we found the proportion of mothers who were forced to go to work early after confinement quite as high as in former years, and for the same cause—the lack of employment among the men. Wrong addresses are still occasionally given, the reason in many instances being a desire to evade vaccination. "The second Table, No. L., shows the number of revisits—many more than last year—and the method of feeding then found, the average age at the second visit being from four to five months. "Comparing the result of our investigation with regard to feeding at the first visit with that of last year (as shown in Table XLIX.) it is gratifying to note that the number of mothers giving food in addition to the breast is much less. This is a point on which we have laid great stress in our visits, the 'food' being always bread sop or biscuit. The reason the mothers give us is that it soothes the child, sends it to sleep, and 'makes it more contented.' Often young mothers tell us that they have given the food on the advice of neighbours, and are generally willing to discontinue it when we point out to them how unnecessary it is, and also how very risky. There is a foolish fallacy among mothers that breast and cows' milk will not agree. This prejudice is being gradually overcome, as may be seen by the increased number now giving both. There is a growing tendency to use condensed milk, especially during the summer, when there is a difficulty in keeping cows' milk, another reason being the H 102 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. inability through poverty, this year, to buy cows' milk. We are sorry at the same time to note the prevalent habit of dipping the 'comforter' into the tin of condensed milk, it thus becoming a fruitful source of intestinal trouble. "The use of farinaceous food alone is slightly on the decrease. A contrast in the attitude of mothers with regard to patent foods is shown in the following cases :—One was found, at the first visit, to be already following the pamphlet given at the birth of an older child, who was proudly shown as never having had a day's illness, 'and,' the mother added, 'I will never buy patent foods again.' The other was feeding her infant of less than six weeks on condensed milk and a certain patent food. It was impossible to influence her, as she was going in for that particular patent-food prize. "Again, the use of boat bottles is largely on the increase. 315 boat bottles were used (Putney two, Balham 23, Tooting 126, Southfield four, Fairfield 54, Springfield 106), as against 55 tube bottles (Balham six, Tooting 12, Fairfield 13, Springfield 24). Both tube and boat bottles were in use in 11 instances (Balham two, Tooting four, Southfield one, Fairfield 3, Springfield 1), and spoons were used in preference to either in 96 cases (Putney one, Balham 12, Tooting 50, Fa'rfield nine, Springfield 24). Several mothers have told us that they would use the boat shape but that they cannot afford it, every penny being of importance when the husband is so frequently out of work. The large number of spoons used is due to the fact that mothers are often afraid that giving a bottle may wean the baby from the breast. "It has again been difficult to keep in touch with the mothers of illegitimate children. In the Springfield \\ ard one, and in Tooting two, wrong addresses were given, and it was impossible to trace them. 13 had removed immediately after the birth (Putney one, Balham one, Tooting four, Southfield two. Springfield five), and of these, five came into the Borough for the confinement only, and two of the infants had died from prematurity before the mother's removal. Therefore we were only successful in visiting 46 (Balham four, Tooting 19, Southfield five, Fairfield eight, Springfield io), Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 103 and of these, nine had removed before we could visit them a second time. Many of the mothers were at work outside the Borough, but came home to their relatives for the confinement and left the babies to be nursed here when they returned to their situations. "24 were entirely breast-fed at the first visit (Balham one, Tooting six, Southfield five, Fairfield four, Springfield eight), the five in Southfield representing the total number of illegitimate infants visited there. 24 re-visits were made (Balham two, Tooting 10, Fairfield five, Springfield seven), and of these, nine were still entirely breast-fed (Tooting one, Fairfield three, Springfield five). "A very large proportion of illegitimate deaths occurred in Tooting, five out of the seven deaths belonging to that district, the other two being in Balham and Fairfield. None of the illegitimate children registered in Wandsworth were premature, or delicate from birth, but in Tooting four were delicate, and one was premature, and of these, two died. Pneumonia was the cause of death in two instances (Balham one, Fairfield one). Diarrhoea in three (Tooting), and Erysipelas in one (Tooting); diseases which suggest a certain amount of neglect on the part of the mother or nurse—and Prematurity one (Tooting). "We had the help of a voluntary worker at intervals during the year, at first to make good arrears of work caused by absence through illness during the whole of February and March. When the work was brought up to date she devoted her attention to the Southfield Ward, hitherto untouched, and later we made a beginning in Putney, but shortly after that she left to take up an official position and we had to restrict our work to its former limits. "With regard to the employment of midwives. Out of the 1,996 mothers visited, 807 availed themselves of their services. Of these, 528 employed midwives registered under the London County Council (Putney 11, Balham 49, Tooting 169, Southfield 16, Fairfield 69, Springfield 214). The non- 104 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. registered midwives were employed chiefly by the very poor (Putney two, Balham 40, Tooting 100, Southfield 20, Fairfield 57, Springfield 60). "The sanitary defects discovered in the course of our visits were remedied, or, if involving structural work, leferred to you. In Balham 21 intimation notices were served, in Tooting 66, in Wandsworth 65 (Southfield six, Fairfield 31, Springfield 28). Three statutory notices were requited in Balham, seven in Tooting, and four in Wandsworth (Fairfield one, Springfield three)." ios Report of the Medical Officer of Health. TABLE XLIX. Of the' 1,996 infants visited, it was found that 1,601, or 8o'2 per cent., were wholly breast-fed, while 395, or ig"8 per cent., were otherwise fed. If, however, we add to the number wholly breast-fed the number of children in which other foods were given in addition we find that the percentage is increased to 89" 1, a slight increase compared with the previous years. 112, or 5.6 per cent., were fed on cows' milk only, but 22 had other foods in addition, raising the percentage to 6.7, a decrease compared with 1907, when the percentage was 7.1. 62 had condensed milk only, and nine had condensed milk and food, a total of 71, or 3'5 per cent., an increase of '4 per cent, compared with 1907. Eleven infants were led on farinaceous food only, compared with nine in the previous year. With reference to the cases re-visited, it was found that out of a total of 1,098, 726 or 66.7 per cent, were still breast-fed only, compared with 64'6 per cent, in 1907; while taking other foods with the breast the percentage was nearly 77. The total number of deaths among those infants visited by the Inspectors was 66, and of these only 20 died from Diarrhoea or Enteritis. As stated by the Inspectors, further inquiry after death showed that of these 20, five were delicate from birth, two were twins, one was the eldest of triplets, and three were entirely breast-fed. The mortality rate among the infants visited was thus only 33 per 1,000, compared with 98 over the whole Borough. As in former years the visits of the Inspectors are almost wholly confined to the poorer classes among whom this mortality is high. 108 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of these, eight were stated to have been due to Septicaemia or Pyasmia, three to Ulcerative or Malignant Endocarditis, and one to Glanders. The death caused by Glanders was a male of 20 years, and as he was a clerk and not engaged in looking after horses it was difficult to trace the source of infection. The only source of infection that could be ascertained was that he, when playing at a certain cricket match, stroked a horse in the cricket field, which afterwards was found to be suffering from the disease. This information was not obtained until after the inquest. 109 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The next Table, which appears in this Report for the first time, shows the deaths and death-rates in males and females from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, and for other Tubercular diseases, as well as the deaths and death-rates among children under five years from other Tubercular diseases, for the 10 years 1898-1907, and for the year 1908. As a comparative Table it is of great value as it shows clearly where the greatest reduction in mortality has occurred. Among 1 118 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. males the rate for 1908 was 1.08, compared with 1.19 the decennial average, a reduction of .11 per 1,000, but among females the rate was only .57, compared with .8o, a reduction of .23 per 1,000, or more than double the reduction among males. Again, from other forms of Tubercular diseases the rate for 1908 was, among males .34, compared with .47, the decennial average, while among females the rate was .31, compared with .31, the decennial average, showing that from other forms of Tubercular diseases the reduction in mortality has been almost wholly among males. Among children under five years the rate was .20, compared with .26, the decennial average, only a slight reduction. Why the death-rate among females should show this marked reduction is due in all probability to the fact that housing conditions have improved, that home infection is less frequent owing to the removal of cases to hospitals, infirmaries, and sanatoria, and consequent less personal nursing. The smaller reduction in the mortality among males is probably due to their greater exposure to outside infection, to overfatigue, alcoholic excess, and confinement in badly ventilated offices and workshops. 119 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Voluntary Notification of Consumption. During the year 85 cases of Consumption were notified bymedical practitioners, seven from Clapham, six from Putney, seven from Streatham, 10 from Tooting, and 55 from Wandsworth, compared with 49 in 1907, 74 in 1906, 104 in 1905, 118 in 1904, and 88 in 1903. Three of the cases were from five to 15 years of age, and 63 from 25 to 65 years. Of the 85 cases notified, 10 were notified from the Workhouse, 15 from Brompton Hospital, two from St. Thomas's Hospital, and two were notified on discharge from the Army. In five of the cases no inquiries were made at the request of the medical attendant, and in three of the cases notified from the Brompton Hospital wrong addresses were given. 25 of the cases died, 12 were removed to the Infirmary, and one left the Borough, leaving 29 still under observation. At the close of 1907 there were still 11 cases under observation, and during 1908 six of these removed to other places outside the Borough, and one was removed to the Union Infirmary, leaving four of these cases still under observation. Of these four, one was notified in 1904, one in 1905, one in 1906, and one in 1907. The total number at the close of 1908 on the Register under observation is thus 33. In all 97 visits were made during the year by the female Sanitary Inspectors in connection with cases of Consumption. In 18 of the cases visited for the first time during the year a definite history of infection by a previous case in the family was obtained. The next Table shows the number of cases reported, exclusive of those reported from external Institutions, from 1903 to 1908. 121 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the total number, viz.:—432, 230 have died, 122 removed to other districts, 23 were stated to have recovered, six were stated after inquiry not to be suffering from Consumption, in 31 cases no inquiries were made, leaving 20 cases still living at the close of 1908. For statistical purposes it is unsatisfactory that the number of removals was so large, but in the majority of these cases it was impossible to ascertain the address to which the patient had gone. During the current year this will to a large extent be obviated, as the system of notification in force by District Medical Officers and Medical Officers of Poor Law Institutions, in combination with the notification of change of address by the Relieving Officer 'will, it is hoped, diminish the numbers under this heading. Bacteriological Examination of Sputum. 72 examinations of sputum were made at the Lister Institute, six from Clapham, four from Putney, 23 from Streatham, five from Tooting, and 34 from Wandsworth. In 21 of the cases it was reported that the bacillus had been found, and in 51 that it was absent. Circulatory Diseases. From the above group of diseases 350 deaths occurred, 153 of which, 69 males and 84 females, were due to Organic disease of the Heart. 84 of these deaths occurred in Clapham, 36 in Putney, 103 in Streatham, 33 in Tooting, and 94 in Wandsworth. Of the deaths from Organic diseases of the Heart, one was under one year, three were from one to five years, four from five to 15 years, 14 from 15 to 25 years, 71 from 25 to 65 years, and 60 over 65 years. The total number of deaths was 543, compared with 550 in 1907 511 in 1906, and 512 in 1905. The number of deaths was seven less than in the previous year, but 119 less than the corrected decennial average. 128 of the deaths occurred in Clapham, 41 in Putney, 98 in Streatham, 73 in Tooting, and 203 in Wandsworth. Compared with 1907 there is an increase in Clapham and Tooting, and a decrease in Putney, Streatham and Wandsworth. 139 of the deaths occurred from Acute Bronchitis, compared with 176 in 1907; 127 from Chronic Bronchitis, compared with 113; 97 from Lobar Pneumonia, compared with 100; 149 from Lobular or Broncho-Pneumonia, compared with 135; and 31 from other diseases of the Respiratory Organs, compared with 26. 137 of the deaths were of infants under one year, 58 of persons from one to five years, one from five to 15 years, six from 15 to 25 years, 114 from 25 to 65 years, and 199 over 65 years. The greatest number of deaths from these diseases occurred, as in previous years, among infants under one year and persons over 65 years. From Alcoholism, Acute and Chronic, 12 deaths, five of males and seven of females, occurred ; and 32, 18 of males and 14 of females, from Cirrhosis of the Liver, compared with 11 and 36 in 1907. From Nephritis and Bright's disease 128 deaths occurred, 66 of males and 62 of females, compared with 125 in 1907. From Accidents and diseases of Parturition 15 deaths occurred, all from 25 to 65 years. This, added to the number of deaths from Puerperal Fever, viz.:—6, gives a total of 21 deaths after childbirth, or a proportion of 2.8 per 1,000 births. In England and Wales for the year 1907, which is the latest available report, this rate was 3.83, and in the 10 years immediately preceding the average proportion had been 4.39 per 1,000 births. Of the 15. deaths three occurred in Clapham, eight in Streatham, two in Tooting, and two in Wandsworth. Nine of the deaths were due to Cardiac Syncope, three to Convulsions, two to Extra-uterine Gestation, and one to Haemorrhage. From Acute Appendicitis there were 17 deaths during the year, four in Clapham, three in Putney, six in Streatham, and four in Wandsworth. One case of Lead Poisoning was reported, the patient, a man, aged 38 years, having been a labourer at a type works. 127 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Deaths and Causes of Death in Public Institutions. In the Public Institutions in the Borough 818 deaths occurred during the year, compared with 807 in 1907, 771 in 1906, 729 in 1905, and 669 in 1904. Of these 633 deaths were of persons not belonging to the Borough, compared with 660 in 1907, and 185 of persons belonging to the Borough, compared with 147 in 1907. The increase was due to the increase in the number of deaths of persons belonging to the Borough. In Clapham 55 deaths occurred in the Hostel of God, six of these being Borough residents. Of these 30 were due to Cancer, 21 to Tubercular disease of the Lungs, one from Organic disease of the Heart, one from other Tubercular disease, one from Old Age, and one from other causes. Seven deaths occurred in the Priory, Roehampton, two from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, two from Old Age, and three from other causes. In Streatham there were 18 deaths in St. Anne's Home and six in the Middlesex County Asylum Annexe. Of the 18 in St. Anne's Home one was from Influenza, nine were from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, one from Old Age, one from Bright's disease, one from Accident, and five from other causes. Of the six in the Middlesex County Asylum Annexe two were from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, one from other forms of Tubercular diseases, one from Organic disease of the Heart, and two from other causes. In Tooting 535 deaths occurred in Public Institutions, 130 of which were of persons belonging to the Borough. Of the 268 deaths in the Tooting Bee Asylum, 115 were from Old Age, 10 from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, 48 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, 22 from diseases of the Heart, 33 from diseases of the Nervous System, 11 from Bright's disease, 10 from Cancer, four from Accident, and 15 from all other causes. 132 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In the Fever Hospitals 126 deaths occurred, 48 of which belonged to the Borough, compared with 53 in 1907, 55 in 1906, 50 in 1905, and 106 in 1904. 63 of these deaths were from Scarlet Fever, 51 from Diphtheria, none from Enteric Fever, one from Measles, two from Tubercular diseases, one from Alcoholism, three from Developmental diseases, one from Organic disease of the Heart, one from Accident, and three from all other causes. 132 deaths occurred in the Tooting Home, 64 of which were Borough residents. 45 of these were from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, 37 from Old Age, 22 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, six from Cancer, two from Organic disease of the Heart, two from Influenza, one from Accident, and 17 from all other causes. In Wandsworth 85 deaths occurred in the Middlesex County Asylum, 12 in the Royal Hospital for Incurables, five in the Prison, and 23 in the Workhouse. Of the 85 deaths in the Middlesex County Asylum, 11 were from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, 13 from Old Age, nine from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, seven from Bright's disease, two from Enteritis, two from other forms of Tubercular diseases, 30 from diseases of the Brain and Nervous System (of which 21 were from General Paralysis of the Insane, and three from Apoplexy), one from Obstruction of Intestines, and 10 from other causes. Of the 12 deaths in the Royal Hospital for Incurables, one was from Influenza, two from Cancer, two from Chronic Bronchitis, one from Old Age, and six from all other causes. In other places not Institutions 87 deaths occurred, 15 of these being from Accidents, three from Cancer, eight from Suicide, four from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, two from disease of the Heart, eight from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, four from Old Age, three from Nephritis, one from Influenza, one from Erysipelas, and 19 from all other causes. 27 of these deaths occurred in Private Nursing Homes, and eight in the River Thames. There has been a marked increase in the number of deaths in Institutions, and to show that the increase has been going on for for some years the following Table has been prepared. The comparative percentages for London are also shown, and it will be noted that in the 20 years 1888-1907 this increased in London from 23.6 to 39.8, with an average of 30.2, and in Wandsworth from 15.2 to 25.9, with an average of 18.6. In this Borough the percentage is lower than for the whole of London, and this is what might be expected in consequence of the social condition of the population, but in spite of the better social condition it is rather startling to find that over 25 per cent, of the total deaths occurred in Institutions, and that for the year 1908 12. per cent, occurred in Pauper Institutions. Compared with the 20 years' average the greatest increase has occurred among the deaths in General and Special Hospitals, while the deaths in the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals show no marked difference.
