Cutting-off Notices. During the year 60 notices with respect to 75 premises were received from the Water Board stating that the supply had been withdrawn. 37 of these notices referred to premises in the Lambeth District supply, and 23 in the Southwark District supply. Five of the premises were in Clapham, 12 in Putney, 34 in Streatham, 16 in Tooting, and eight in Wandsworth. The supply was withdrawn for non-payment of rate from 31 premises, from 26 premises in consequence of defects, in 16 the premises were empty, and in two the supply was withdrawn by request. Compared with 1907 there has been a considerable diminution in the number of notices received. There has been a diminution in the number of notices received in consequence of non-payment of rate, but an increase in the number where defects in the service existed. Mortuaries. No change has taken place in the four Mortuaries during the year. I have inspected them on several occasions and am able to report that the Regulations and By-laws made by the Council in 1905 have been in all cases complied with. The site of the new Mortuary for Wandsworth has not yet been decided on, and the matter is still under the consideration of the Public Health Committee. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 137 In Clapham 51 bodies were removed to the Mortuary, compared with 33 in 1907 ; in Streatham 38, compared with 49; and in Wandsworth 115, of which 56 were from Wandsworth, 29 from Putney, and 30 from Tooting; a total of 204, compared with 171 in 1907. General Sanitary Work in 1908. The following list shows the special inspections made by me during the year :— (1.) By request of the Licensing Justices the Council instructed me to inspect and report on the following public-houses, and also to give evidence before the Compensation Committee at the Sessions :— (a) "The Turk's Head," Garratt Lane, Wandsworth. (b) "The Horse and Groom," Garratt Lane, Wandsworth. (c) "The Sir Geoffrey Dunstan," Summerstown, Wandsworth. (2.) In consequence of a communication from the Local Government Board with regard to a letter received by them complaining of an open drain on Wimbledon Common, I made an inspection of all the ponds and ditches of that part of the Common which is situated in this Borough, and also of those on Putney Heath. No nuisance of any kind was discovered, and with respect to the particular drain complained of the following report was made :— 6th February, 1908. "In reply to the extract from Dr. Denny's letter to the Local Government Board with regard to the open drain from Kingsmere Lake, on Wimbledon Common, I have to report that Kingsmere is drained by a ditch into Scio Pond, from Scio Pond there is a ditch running to the way-side pond in Roehampton Lane, the overflow from which flows into a drain carried along Roehampton Lane, 138 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. then partly by drain and by open ditch through Manresa House grounds, Mount Clare grounds, and Richmond Park to Beverley Brook. The overflow from the Chelsea Water Works Reservoir flows into the ditch between Scio Pond and the way-side pond. "There is at no point in the course of this drain any connection with the sewage system, and no sewage from any of the houses enters the drain. The way-side pond in Roehampton Lane is 200 yards from the Infants' School, and was, at my request, cleaned out by the Conservators at the beginning of October, 1907. I inspected the pond at the time it was being cleaned out, and the material taken out consisted wholly of vegetable matter and earth which had been washed from the road and banks. "In my previous report on the outbreak at Roehampton I stated that all the drains, sewers and gullies had been inspected and thoroughly flushed, also that the drains at the school had been tested with water with a satisfactory result and a certificate granted in 1907." (3.) Special inspections were made of all the premises where offensive trades were carried on, for the purpose of examining the means adopted to comply with the London County Council By-laws as to the ventilation of such premises. The three premises dealt with during the year were :— (1.) Harrison, Barber & Co., Garratt Lane, Wandsworth. (2.) Ford's, Bendon Valley, Wandsworth. (3.) Dallett's, High Street, Putney. In all these some system of mechanical ventilation had been installed, and I am able to report that the By-laws have now been complied with. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 139 (4.) Special inspections were made on several occasions of the River Wandle, and some gaugings were taken for the purpose of ascertaining the normal discharge of the river. These gaugings were taken as a Bill had been introduced into Parliament to prohibit the Croydon Corporation from extracting more water from the Wandle at its source. The River Wandle enters this Borough at Summerstown, and for over half-a-mile forms the boundary between it and Wimbledon. For the one-and-a-half miles to the River Thames it is wholly in this Borough. On several occasions during the last 15 years I have called the attention of the Council to its polluted condition, and to the foul deposits which accumulate in the bed of the river in its whole course in the Borough. This deposit is, in my opinion, increasing, owing very largely to two causes : 1st, the greater amount of sewage which is discharged from the four outfalls, viz. :—those of Croydon Corporation, Carshalton District Council, Croydon Rural Council, and Wimbledon Corporation, this sewage containing a considerable amount of suspended matter; and 2nd, to a diminution of water in the river, this being caused by the lessened amount of water finding its way into the river from its natural gathering grounds owing to the great increase in the number of houses in Tooting and Wandsworth. There has also been a diminution in the amount of water from the River Graveney owing to the same cause. Roughly speaking, one sixth of the whole volume of the river water from Wickens-Pease's works consists of water from sewage farms, and I am decidedly of opinion that any further abstraction of water from the Wandle at its source would cause an increase in the amount of solid matter and therefore greater pollution. On the 2nd July I gauged the flow of the river at Penwith Road bridge, and I calculated that at the time the 143 Report,of the Medical Officer of Health. charge was only a little over 30 million gallons in the 24 hours, a considerable diminution from the gaugings made about the same place in the year 1851, which gave a discharge of over 80 million gallons. (5.) Special inspections were again made this year of several localities in consequence of complaints of smells from sewer ventilators. These complaints were more numerous in 1908 than in 1907, chiefly on account of the increase in the number received from Putney. In all 143 complaints were received, six from Clapham North, 10 from Clapham South, 33 from Putney, 24 from Streatham, eight from Balham, 11 from Tooting, 24 from Southfield, eight from Fairfield, and 19 from Springfield Wards. All these complaints were forwarded to the Borough Engineer and Surveyor, who reported that in every case the necessary cleansing and flushing or other work had been carried out. (6.) Numerous inspections were again made of several mews and passages during the year, and it is satisfactory to be able to report that several of these have been effectually dealt with, while others will be completed during the early part of the current year. (a) Passage in rear of 122, 124, 126, Upper Tooting Road, paved. (b) Passage in rear of 1-16, The Parade, Tooting, paved. (c) Passage in rear of 17-24, The Parade, Tooting, paved. (d) Passage between 41 and 43, High Street, and in rear of 43 and 45, High Street, Tooting, paved. Report of the Medical Officer of' Health. 141 (e) Passage off Montserrat Road, and in rear of 97-109, High Street, Putney, paved. (f) Passage off Disraeli Road in rear of 137-151, High Street, Putney, paved. (g) With reference to the passage at the rear of 152-180, Streatham High Road, as no work was being done to abate the nuisance proceedings were taken against the owners, but before the hearing of the summonses work was begun and this will shortly be satisfactorily carried out. (h) Road between 205 and 207, Brixton Hill. The condition of this road has been the cause of several complaints by the occupiers of houses in Holmwood Gardens, and many difficulties have arisen with respect to ownership and consequent responsibility as to the persons liable to abate the nuisance. A satisfactory settlement has, however, now been arrived at, and I anticipate that in the spring of the current year the road will be properly made up and the nuisance abated. (i) Field's Alley, Wandsworth, is still in hand, but the necessary paving has not been done, owing chiefly to change of ownership. This also will be paved satisfactorily during the current year. (k) With regard to land at the rear of Blackshaw Road, owing to the difficulty of ascertaining the owners as defined by the Public Health Act, nothing has been done, but as soon as the actual owners are found the necessary notices will be served. (7.) In consequence of complaints from the tenants of the houses in Floss Street, Putney, I made an inspection of them on the 4th May, 1908, and reported to the Committee that, in my opinion, the basement tenements were at that time unfit for habitation in consequence of an accumulation of water in the 142 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. yards, which extended into several of the basements. Notices were served on the owners, and although some of the occupiers vacated the houses, no work was done to permanently abate the nuisance. The matter came before the magistrate and was adjourned, as the owners promised to arrange with the Ranelagh Club, whose premises adjoin the houses, to construct a drain to the Beverley Brook. This was done and all the water was satisfactorily drained away. The subsoil and the basements still continued damp and I advised the owners to construct a drain in the yards to discharge into the drain which had been made by the Ranelagh Club, and at present this is being done. The owners have also carried out a considerable amount of other work in the houses themselves, sothat they will, during the current year, be again rendered fit for habitation. (8.) Special inspections were made in Fairfield and part of Southfield Wards for the purpose of ascertaining the number of empty houses in these districts available for the working classes. This inspection was necessary to enable me to give evidence at the Local Government Board Inquiry into the demolition of houses in Fairfield Ward by the Wandsworthand Putney Gas Light and Coke Company. The Table of Sanitary Operations shows in detail the amount and nature of the work carried out by the Sanitary Inspectors during the year. The number of complaints received and attended to was 1,645, compared with 1,836 in 1907, 1,595 in 1906, and 1,357 in 1905. There was an increase in Putney, Balham, Tooting, and Southfield Wards, and a decrease in both Wards in Clapham, Streatham, Springfield, and Fairfield Wards. 13 of these complaints were made to the London County Council, and these were all at once attended to. The number of houses and premises inspected was 24,892, an increase of 662 compared with the previous year. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 145 The total number of house-to-house inspections made was 5)575) compared with 5,718 in 1907, 5,686 in 1906, 6,290 in 1905, and 6,440 in 1904. There has been an increase in the number of house-to-house inspections in the Putney, Streatham, Balham, and Springfield Wards, and a decrease in Clapham North and South, and Tooting Wards. The decrease in house-to-house inspections which, however, was not great, was due partly to the great amount of illness from which members of the staff suffered during the year. This was altogether due to Influenza, which attacked nearly every member of the staff in one form or another. The decrease was also partly due to the increased amount of necessary work to be carried out by the Inspectors, and to the amount of infectious diseases that occurred in some of the districts. At the close of the year an additional Sanitary Inspector was appointed, and I anticipate that during 1909 there will be an increase in the amount of house-tohouse inspections, especially in Tooting and Streatham Wards. 146 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In 3,303 out of a total of 5,575 houses inspected, or 59.24 per cent., sanitary defects necessitating the service of an intimation notice were found. The highest percentage was in the Streatham Ward, where the percentage was 72.02, and the lowest in Southfield Ward, where it was only 42.97, the next lowest being Clapham North Ward, where it was 48.40. Clapham, North 721 349 48.40 97 180 133 559 ,, South 550 366 66.54 68 98 278 355 Putney 830 410 49.39 7 60 312 601 Streatham 404 291 72.02 46 73 175 271 Balham 748 487 65.10 95 197 371 552 Tooting 226 146 64.60 >9 32 71 15'1 Southfield 747 321 42.97 76 52 268 553 Fairfield 592 394 66-55 60 115 182 445 Springfield 757 539 71.20 119 102 496 436 Whole Borough 5575 3303 59.24 587 909 2286 3923 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 159 The total number of intimation notices served was 8,138, compared with 7,613 in 1907 and 7,500 in 1906, and of statutory notices 1,564, compared with 1,100 and 1,044. The greatest number of intimation notices was served in Balham and Spring-field Wards, and the smallest number in Southfield and Streatham Wards. The greatest number of statutory notices was served in Balham and Springfield Wards, and the smallest number in Putney and Fairfield Wards. 154 of the statutory notices served in 1908 were not complied with. The majority of these were served in December and were complied with in the early months of the current year. The number of statutory notices served include By-law notices, 486 of which were served in respect of the following contraventions of the By-laws:—Yard paving 174, repairs to water closets 79, repairs to flushing apparatus of water closets 58, covers to cisterns 30, cleansing cisterns 44, cleansing house and other contraventions of the By-laws as to houses let in lodgings 93, paving and draining stables three, depositing house refuse within prohibited distance two, and absence of proper covers to manure receptacles three. The number of cases of overcrowding dealt with during the year was 158, compared with 87 in 1907, 100 in 1906, and 92 in 1905. 12 of these were in Clapham, two in Putney, 37 in Streatham, six in Tooting, and 101 in Wandsworth. The cases of overcrowding in Clapham were chiefly in Rashleigh Street, Westbury Street, and Lower Orchard Street; in Putney, in Quill Lane and The Platt; in Streatham, in Factory Square, Fallsbrook Road, Larch Road, Zennor Road, Triangle Flats, Foss Road, and Hazlehurst Road; in Tooting, in Aldis Mews; and in Wandsworth, in Aslett Street, Guelph Street, Bendon Valley, Lydden Grove, and Wardlev Street. 1,233 ashpits were provided or repaired, compared with 1,099 in 1907, and 1,219 in 1906, and 271 complaints with reference to the non-removal of house refuse (36 from Clapham, 34 from 160 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Putney, 93 from Streatham, 22 from Tooting, and 86 from Wandsworth) were received during the year. These complaints were as in previous years attended to by the Dusting Inspectors. Removal of House Refuse. No change has taken place in the methods of removal or disposal of house refuse during the year, details of which have been given in previous reports. During the 12 months ending March, 1908, the quantity of dust collected and disposed of was approximately 58,623 tons, compared with 53,536 tons in the previous year, and the total cost of collection and disposal was £16,288, compared with ,£17,076. 172 statutory notices were served under section 37 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, to provide ashpits in accordance with the By-laws, and these have been complied with. 1,025 yards in connection with dwelling houses, and 135 in connection with stables, were paved, drained or cleansed, compared with 1,084 and* 84 in 1907. 5,856 rooms and 1,327 staircases and passages were cleansed or repaired, compared with 5,456 rooms and 945 staircases and passages in 1907. The greatest number of rooms cleansed was in the Balham Ward, where 1,266 were done, and in Springfield Ward, where 1,127 wee done, and the smallest number in Putney and Southfield Wards. 1,272 drains were cleansed or repaired and 99 rain-water pipes disconnected from the drains. 124 notices of reconstruction of drains were sent to the Borough Surveyor from this Department: 18 in Clapham, Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 161 pared with 25 in 1907; nine in Putney, compared with five; 41 in Streatham, compared with 70; 12 in Tooting, compared with 12 ; and 44 in Wandsworth, compared with 45. The total number of houses where the drains were reconstructed during the year was 289, 43 in Clapham, 16 in Putney, 106 in Streatham, 30 in Tooting, and 94 in Wandsworth. 361 accumulations of manure were removed after service of notice, compared with 266 in 1907, 59 in Clapham, 46 in Putney, 159 in Streatham, 40 in Tooting, and 57 in Wandsworth. There has been an increase in the number of cases dealt with in all the sub-districts. 55 notices were served for the periodical removal of the manure every 48 hours, compared with 33 in 1907, 16 in 1906, and 20 in 1905. The number of water closets cleansed and repaired was 2,128, compared with 1,809 in 1907, and 1,936 in 1906. The greatest number of these were in Balham and Springfield Wards. 755 drinking water cisterns were cleansed, covered or repaired, 160 in Clapham, 61 in Putney, 117 in Streatham, 24 in Tooting, and 393 in Wandsworth, compared with 743 in 1907. 90 premises were inspected and notices served where animals were kept in such a manner as to be a nuisance. 10 of these were in Clapham, two in Putney, 34 in Streatham, six in Tooting, and 38 in Wandsworth. This nuisance was most common in Streatham and Springfield Wards, Wandsworth, and the animals kept were chiefly ducks and hens. 90 smoke nuisances were reported during the year, three in Clapham, nine in Putney, nine in Streatham, two in Tooting, and 67 in Wandsworth. Proceedings were taken in one case during the year, and a fine of 20s. was inflicted and 7s. costs. 162 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 15 complaints of black smoke were received from the Public Control Department of the London County Council with regard to 22 premises, and 13 complaints from the Coal Smoke Abatement Society with regard to 21 premises. These complaints were all at once attended to and the premises kept under observation. 125 soil pipes were repaired, 16 in Clapham, 18 in Putney 58 in Streatham, one in Tooting, and 32 in Wandsworth, compared with 184 in 1907, and 185 in 1906. 945 leaky roofs and gutters were repaired, compared with 812 in 1907. 97 unwholesome or dilapidated houses were cleansed and repaired, 30 in Clapham, 15 in Putney, 12 in Streatham, 19 in Tooting, and 21 in Wandsworth, compared with 56 in 1907. The number of unclassified nuisances was 863, 229 in Clapham, 92 in Putney, 196 in Streatham, 130 in Tooting, and 216 in Wandsworth, compared with 758 in 1907. Disinfection. The total number of rooms disinfected after the occurrence of cases of infectious disease was 2,868 (497 in Clapham, 140 in Putney, 788 in Streatham, 495 in Tooting, and 948 in Wandsworth), compared with 3,206 in 1907, 2,794 in 1906, and 2,947 in 1905. The decrease was chiefly due to the decrease in the number of cases of Scarlet Fever. 1,826 lots of bedding were stoved at the Disinfecting Station, compared with 1,948 in 1907, 1,669 in 1906 and 1,778 in 1905. The 1,826 lots of bedding included the following —1,301 beds, 1,290 bolsters, 2,856 pillows, 1,219 mattresses, 505 palliasses, Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 163 2,227 sheets, 1,983 quilts, 736 cushions, 5,730 articles of clothing-, 3,608 blankets, 543 carpets, and 2,236 extra articles, a total of 24,034 articles, compared with 29,102 in 1907, 28,885 in 1906, and 30,477 in 1905. 884 premises were disinfected after Measles and 184 after Tubercular disease of the Lungs, compared with 955 and 135 in 1907. 22 beds, 11 bolsters, 32 pillows, 36 mattresses, 39 palliasses, eight sheets, eight quilts, 27 cushions, 88 articles of clothing, 18 blankets, 11 carpets, and 159 extra articles, were removed and destroyed by request of the owners during the year. Disinfection was also carried out by the Sanitary Staff at premises after non-notifiable diseases, chiefly Cancer, and the sum of £18 8s. 6d. was received for such disinfection. 66 rooms were disinfected in consequence of their verminous condition, 18 rooms were sprayed, and 16 lots of verminous bedding disinfected. The disinfection by steam was carried out at the Station at Tooting, and I am pleased to report that this Station has proved most satisfactory in every way. I have regularly visited the Station during the year, and am able to report that the work has been carried out by the staff to my entire satisfaction. Only two complaints of damage to goods by disinfection have been received, and on inquiry it was found that the goods were damaged or stained before being placed in the disinfecting chamber. The approximate cost of working and maintaining the Disinfecting Station during the past year, including the cost of disinfectants used in fumigating and cleansing rooms, was as follows — 164 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. £ 5. d. Wages 917 15 9 Fuel !5 2 0 Horse Hire 132 4 0 Stores, Tools, and other materials 61 5 8 Repairs to Horse Vans and Harness ... 14 1 0 Disinfectants, etc. 33 18 3 Repairs to Motor Van ... 53 1 10 Motor Running Expenses 17 6 8 Lighting 1 10 3 Insurance 10 0 6 Water 4 0 5 £1,260 6 4 The number of lots of bedding stoved was 1,826. Cost per lot of bedding 13s. 9d. Cleansing of Persons Act. The baths at the Disinfecting Station have been more used this year, chiefly owing to the number of adults dealt with. The names of 125 children, stated to be in a verminous condition, were forwarded to me by the Medical Officer, Education Department, London County Council, and the homes were visited by the Inspectors. In the majority of cases only the heads of the children were verminous, but in a considerable number the bodies and clothing were also verminous. Of these latter, 29, 19 boys and 10 girls, were cleansed at the baths, and the clothing was at the same time stoved with beneficial results. 12 adults also had baths, two being nurses who had been in attendance on cases of Puerperal Fever, six were contacts with cases of Scarlet Fever, while four, three males and one female, were verminous. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 165 Inspection of Urinals in connection with Licensed Premises. The number of urinals inspected was 127, 36 in Clapham, 20 in Putney, 27 in Streatham, 14 in Tooting-, and 40 in Wandsworth. 366 inspections were made during the year and 64 notices, chiefly for cleansing, were served and complied with. Police Court Proceedings. The list* which follows shows particulars of Police Court proceedings under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, during the year. The total amount of fines inflicted was £27 4s., and the costs £11 11s., compared with £99 8s. 6d. and £14 6s. 5d. in 1907. The majority of the summonses served were with respect to nuisances or contravention of By-laws, but three of the cases were for permitting a house to be occupied without first having obtained a certificate from the Sanitary Authority that the premises had a proper and sufficient supply of water. Two were for exposing unsound food for sale, one for carrying carcase of a horse through the street in an improper vehicle, and one for carrying on the business of a purveyor of milk without being registered. At the close of 1907 there were 364 milkshops on the Register, 37 have been added in 1908 and 34 removed, leaving 367 on the Register at the end of 1908. During the year 874 inspections were made of these premises and 59 notices, chiefly for cleansing, were served. One prosecution was instituted for carrying on the business of a dairyman without being registered and a fine of 8s. was imposed with 7s. costs. 170 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Special attention has again been given this year to the enforcing of the Milkshop Regulations, and the notice of all registered purveyors of milk was called to the regulations which deal with the protection of milk against infection or contamination. Very important and necessary powers have been given to Sanitary Authorities by the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908, Section 5, to remove from or refuse to enter on the Register the name of persons carrying on the trade of cowkeepers or dairymen or purveyors of milk upon premises which are, in the opinion of such Sanitary Authority, for any reason unsuitable for the sale of milk therein. During the present year a special inspection will be made of all these premises and a special report made on the subject. Cowsheds. There were 20 Slaughter-houses and one Knacker's Yard on the Register in 1907, one in Wandsworth was removed, leaving 20 on the Register. During the year 365 inspections were made of these, but it was only necessary to serve three notices for cleansing. The usual cleansing was carried out by the occupiers at the proper times. During the year the Inspectors inspected the carcases of 300 cattle, 2,221 sheep, 215 pigs, and 46 calves in Slaughter-houses and Butcher's Shops, and reported that all the animals were healthy. 172 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Ice Cream Premises. At the end of 1907 there were 235 of these on the Register. During 1908 47 were added and 31 removed, leaving a total of 251 on the Register. 345 inspections were made of these premises during the year and 23 notices were served, all of which were for cleansing. Houses Let in Lodgings. At the end of 1907 there were 229 houses let in lodgings on the Register. 39 were added during 1908 and one removed, making a total of 267 on the Register at the end of 1908. 705 inspections of these were made during the year, and 434 notices were served, 17 for overcrowding and 417 for other conditions, chiefly cleansing. With regard to one premises a summons was issued for failing to cleanse, and a conviction was obtained. 175 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. No difficulties have arisen during the year in carrying out these By-laws, and I am again able to report that all these premises have been kept in much better condition since being placed on the Register. Offensive Trade Refuse. In June I made the following report to the Public Health Committee with regard specially to the removal of fish offal— "25th June, 1908. "On the 21 st May, 1908, I received a report from one of the Sanitary Inspectors that at 7.40 a.m. on that date he saw a four-wheeled van, with a large iron tank inside, proceeding along Upper Richmond Road, the smell from the van being most offensive. It stopped at the rear entrance in Charlwood Road of the fishmongers at 306, Upper Richmond Road. The Inspector examined the tank and found it was covered with sacking over the rear half, the other half being uncovered. The tank was full of fish offal. The name painted on the van was Fred. S. Hempleman, Crow's Road, West Ham, and the driver's name and address was Henry Monk, 20, Ranleigh Road, West Ham. On receipt of this report I communicated with Messrs. Hempleman, who asked for an appointment to talk over the whole matter. "Messrs. Hempleman's representative called on the 25th May and informed me that it had always been their practice since the beginning of the contract to collect the fish offal from seven premises in Putney with an open van, as they were aware that this method of collection was carried out in Putney by other contractors. "1 have had an inspection made of all the premises in the Borough from which fish offal is removed, and of 122 the offal is removed by Messrs. Hempleman in 82, and in 40 by other contractors. Out of the 82 premises from which it is removed by Messrs. Hempleman, in 75 it is removed in tins as agreed on and in seven by open van. In the 40 premises 176 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. from which this offal is removed by other contractors, in 36it is removed by open vans and in four by tins. "The following Table shows the method of removal in the different Parishes:— Parish. Removed by Hempleman. Removed by other Contractors. Clapham 20 tins. 7 vans. Putney 4 „ 7 vans. 5 „ 2 tins. Streatham 33 „ 2 „ Tooting 9 „ 1 „ Wandsworth 9 „ 21 „ 2 „ Whole Borough 75 „ 7 „ 36„ 4 „ "The arrangement with Messrs. Hempleman was entered into in 1903, and the following circular was sent to those requiring the removal of offensive trade refuse:— " 'Council House, " 'Wandsworth, S.W., " '20th February, 1904. " 'Sir, " 'Collection of offensive trade refuse. " 'Referring to my circular letter of the 12th June,. 1901, with reference to the removal and disposal of fishmongers' and butchers' offal, the Council has again had the subject under consideration, and I am directed to inform you that, owing to the difficulty experienced in disposing of the offal, the Council has been compelled tomake other arrangements for the service. "A contract has therefore been entered into with Messrs. Hempleman & Co., of West Ham, who undertake to collect and dispose of trade refuse in a similar 177 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. manner to that adopted by the Council, leaving the requisite number of empty tins when removing those containing waste, upon the undermentioned charges, which are practically the same as made by the Council, viz. " 'Where the quantity removed does not exceed three tins per week.—One shilling. " 'Where the quantity exceeds three and does not exceed six tins per week.—Two shillings. " 'Where the quantity exceeds six and does not exceed nine tins per week.—Three shillings " 'Where the quantity exceeds nine and does not exceed twelve tins per week.—Four shillings. " 'Any greater quantity than twelve tins to be charged for at the same rate. " 'Yours obediently, (Signed) " 'Henry George Hii.ls, " 'Town Clerk.' "The only alteration that has been made since that date was that the minimum charge was increased to two shillings instead of one shilling. During the time the contract has been in operation I have had very few complaints with regard to the removal carried out by Messrs. Hempleman, but I am of opinion that it would be advisable for the Council to issue a new circular which could be sent to the 122 occupiers of premises from which such offensive trade refuse requires to be removed. "The removal of fish offal in vans is not an infringement of the By-laws made under Sec. 16 (2.) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, provided that the van is 'constructed and furnished with coverings so as to prevent the escape of any matter or liquid therefrom, and so as to prevent any nuisance arising therefrom.' No pressure can therefore be brought to bear upon the other contractors provided they comply with this By-law, but I think it would be advisable for the occupiers of the premises from which offensive trade 178 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. refuse is removed by contractors other than Messrs. Hempleman, to be informed that such refuse should be stored on their premises in proper receptacles, and that fresh receptacles should be left every time such refuse is removed." Messrs. Hempleman informed me in July that the removal of fish offal from the shops in Putney which was formerly done by means of a tank van was now being carried out by means of proper sealed tins. No other complaints were received during the year with reference to the removal of offensive trade refuse. Unsound Food. During the year 1 box of Catfish, 2 boxes of Codfish, 1 box of Sprats, 1 box of Plaice, 2 boxes of Mackerel, 2 cases of Coalfish, 3 boxes of Herrings, 1 box of Whiting, 1 box of Dabs, 1 box of Soles, 3 boxes of Grapes, 1 case of Bananas, 7 baskets of Currants, 10 boxes of Tomatoes, and 16 baskets of Strawberries, were destroyed at the request of the owners, and the usual certificates granted. Factories and Workshops. Although there has been a slight decrease in the number of inspections of factories and workshops during the year, owing chiefly to illness among members of the staff, yet a large amount of work has been successfully carried out, and I am able to report that the condition of these is generally satisfactory. Outworkers' lists have again been the cause of a considerable amount of correspondence, but the circular issued in 1907 has been productive of beneficial results, a greater proportion of the lists having this year been sent in twice without a reminder. The Tables which follow are similar to those used in previous Reports, and the whole of the work done by both male and female Inspectors is there summarised in a concise form. 179 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of workshops on the Register was 1,804, including 160 retail bakehouses, 149 workshop laundries, 313 dressmakers' and milliners' premises, and 1,182 other workshops, but not including 302 factories, 910 workplaces, and 331 outworkers' premises. Including all factories, workshops, workplaces, and outworkers' premises, there were on the Register at the end of 1908 3,347 premises, compared with 3,281 in 1907, 3,096 in 1906, 3,045 in 1905, 2,987 in 1904, and 2,676 in 1903. The total number of inspections made during the year was 5,801, compared with 6,048 in 1907, 5,997 in 1906, 4,933 in 1905, and 4,946 in 1904. The total number of notices served was 434, 403 intimation and 31 statutory, compared with 453 in 1907, 427 in 1906, 462 in 1905, and 465 in 1904. There was only one prosecution under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, for the emission of black smoke. Under the head of other workshops are included domestic workshops, and under the head of places where food is prepared for sale are included the kitchens of hotels, restaurants, tripe, fried fish and eel shops, and other places where food is prepared for sale. In all 614 inspections were made of 302 factories, and 51 notices, 48 intimation and three statutory, were served. The total number of workshops on the Register was 1,804, 149 of which were workshop laundries, 160 workshop bakehouses, and 1,495 other workshops. The increase in the number of workshop laundries is due to the repeal of Section 103 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901. All laundries where there is employment are now classed as workshops. 180 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In all 3,130 inspections of these were made, compared with 3,148 in 1907, 3,233 in 1906, and 2,628 in 1905, and 216 intimation and 19 statutory notices were served. The total number of workplaces on the Register was 910, and 1,676 inspections were made, while 109 intimation and 10 statutory notices were served. The total number of defects found was 609, including 16 cases in which there was a breach of special sanitary requirements for bakehouses, compared with 545 and 16 in 1907. To abate these nuisances 373 intimation and 32 statutory notices were served, all of which, with the exception of 12, were complied with at the end of the year. The 12 outstanding were complied with, with two exceptions, in the first month of this year. These two exceptions were two laundries which were given up shortly after service of the notices. In all, 609 defects were found and 532 remedied, but only 373 intimation notices were served, as one notice frequently called attention to several defects on the same premises. i8i Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Table which follows and which relates to Home Work is similar to the one in last year's Report, except that the number of notices served on occupiers as to keeping or sending- lists is given. There has been an increase of 18 in the number of lists sent in twice a year, and a decrease of three in the number sent in once a year. 184 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In all, 148 lists were received twice in the year, containing the names and addresses of 38 contractors and 476 workers, and 15 lists once in the year, containing the names and addresses of seven contractors and 17 workers. The total number of addresses received from other Councils was 352, compared with 322 in 1907, and 173 in 1906, and 301 addresses were forwarded to other Councils,, compared with 325 in 1907, and 122 in 1906. The following are the districts to which lists of outworkers were forwarded, and also the number of outworkers:—Battersea 66, Lambeth 59, Stepney 58, Camberwell 17, Merton 14, Fulham 11, Southwark 10, Chelsea seven, Hammersmith six, Bethnal Green and Croydon five each, East Ham, Stratford, Bermondsey, Ilford and City of London four each, Hampstead and Hackney three each, Poplar, Islington, St. Pancras, Westminster, Wimbledon, and Chiswick two each, Willesden, Holborn, Finsbury, Stoke Newington, and Penge one each. 63 names and addresses were forwarded to this Borough in error and are not included in the above Table. The number of notices sent to employers during the year was 140, and these had a most satisfactory result, although in some cases it was also necessary for the Inspector to call and warn them that if such lists were not sent proceedings would be taken. At the end of 1907 there were 290 outworkers' premises on the Register, 102 were added and 61 removed during the year, leaving 331 on the Register. 381 inspections were made of these premises during the year, and 30 notices, all for cleansing, were served and complied with. During the year 22 cases of infectious disease were notified as having occurred in the homes of outworkers, compared with six in 1907, six in 1906, and five in 1905. 185 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. During the past year 1,004 samples of foods and drugs were submitted to the Public Analyst for analysis, compared with 989 in 1907, and 905 in 1906. Of these, 88 or 8.76 per cent. were reported to be adulterated, compared with 8.49 per cent. in 1907, and 9.29 per cent. in 1906. The percentage of milk samples found to be adulterated was 6.9, compared with 10.97 in 1907, and 10.26 in 1906. In sixteen of the adulterated milk samples the Public Health Committee, after considering the amount of adulteration and the circumstances of the sale, decided not to prosecute the vendor, so that the percentage of prosecutions was only 3.8, compared with 5.9 in 1907. In 50 out of the 88 cases in which adulterations were detected proceedings were instituted against the vendor, compared with 52 in 1907. The total amount received in penalties under the above Acts during the year amounted to £82 5s., and the costs to £36 4s. 6d., making a total of £118 9s. 6d., compared with £167 4s. in 1907. 198 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Percentage Adulterated. 1899 602 50 8.3 1900 587 43 7.1 1901 600 69 11.5 1902 584 51 8.7 1903 729 90 12.3 1904 750 85 11.3 1905 583 65 7.6 1906 905 84 9.2 1907 989 84 8.4 1908 1,004 88 8.76 It will be observed that the percentage of adulterations detected during the past three years has practically remained stationary. The system whereby the Inspector is allowed to employ his own assistants continues to work admirably, enabling him to change his agents from time to time as he thinks desirable, and although there are, and must necessarily be, offenders who remain undetected, I am convinced from the methods adopted that the resulting percentage of adulterations, viz.:—8'76, represents as nearly as may be the actual amount of adulteration. Much valuable assistance and information has been given to the Inspector during the year by the officers of adjoining Boroughs, more especially with regard to that class of trader who is increasing in numbers, viz. :—the peripatetic milk vendor and butter hawker, persons who trade here, there, and everywhere with no special trade connections, who are frequently not registered in the Borough, and who have no permanent residence. 202 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Clapham 203 2.9 17 8.3 Putney 103 3.3 10 9.7 Streatham 293 2.8 18 6.1 looting 126 4.0 16 12.7 Wandsworth 279 2.8 27 9.6 Whole Borough 1,004 3.0 88 8.76 19 samples of milk have been taken in course of delivery at the various Public Institutions in the Borough, three of which proved to be adulterated. The system of notifying to the various public bodies the figures of analysis obtained with respect to samples so taken is still continued. Much might be done to prevent the sophistication of milk supplied to Public Institutions by wholesale contractors were some such system adopted as that which is in operation at an Institution under the Metropolitan Asylums Board in this Borough. I am informed that upon the arrival of every consignment of milk at this particular Institution it is personally received by the Steward, samples are taken from each churn, and arc analysed by means of a small centrifugal machine whilst the carman waits. Should any milk fall below the required standard it is not accepted, the carman has to take it away, and in accordance with the terms of the contract the Steward is empowered to purchase what he requires elsewhere and charge the same to the contractor. This system 203 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. has been in operation for some considerable time with very satisfactory results. I am informed that the Metropolitan Asylums Board have decided to extend this practice to each of their Institutions. 30 samples have been procured in course of delivery at the various railway stations in the Borough, only one of which proved upon analysis to be adulterated. In this case the Public Health 'Committee did not consider the amount of adulteration sufficient to warrant proceedings being taken. Of the three samples taken at private premises, one proved to be adulterated. The particulars of this case were as follows:- A., a baker and confectioner in the Borough, was in the habit of obtaining a considerable quantity of butter from a large wholesale firm, for which he paid a good price. After being supplied for a short period he found he could not obtain satisfactory results with his pastry and endeavoured to ascertain the cause. His suspicions were aroused, and he came to the conclusion that it was due to the quality of the butter. The Inspector was called in and arranged to procure a sample as it was delivered. This done it was discovered that the article which was being delivered as butter was really a mixture containing no less than 87 per cent, of margarine. In the case of the potted meats, however, the adulteration was confined to a boron preservative which in no single instance was in such quantity as would permit of proceedings being taken. This, in my opinion, is a very satisfactory 205 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. feature of the work done in connection with the Acts in question. They are of necessity preventive measures and if the manufacturers and wholesale dealers find that active steps are being taken to abolish the use of such preservatives within fair limits, then they must perforce adopt a different system in the preparation of the goods. Whilst referring to the subject of potted goods it may be noted that in one instance where potted beef was asked for and the article served was delivered in a tin bearing the label "Potted Beef," it was found on analysis to consist entirely of bloater paste. With regard to the adulteration detected under the heading of confectionery and jam, viz.:—33 per cent., it must be borne in mind that only three samples were purchased. Again, the sample of black currant jam which was found to be adulterated was taken owing to a communication received from the officer in another Borough, but it was found that although adulterated with 75 per cent. of apple pulp an ambiguously worded disclosure was attached by label to the jar. This the Council's solicitor deemed sufficient to protect the vendor, and advised that a conviction could not be obtained. Of the total number of samples taken, 799 or 78 per cent. were purchased by deputies. The Inspector has had during the year five female and four male assistants, as well as two young girls and two boys. It has been found necessary during the past year to procure more supplementary samples than during the previous years, and in several instances a person has been employed for periods ranging from two to six weeks, obtaining goods at various shops prior to an official sample being taken. This has been done with the view of allowing the assistant engaged to become known to the trader as a customer, but it seems to me to be desirable that some arrangement might be made whereby these supplementary samples could be roughly analysed in order that the Inspector could better form an opinion as to the methods adopted by the trader in question. Official figures relating to such test samples prove that the informal samples thus taken afford a better indication of adulteration than is given by formal sampling. 2o6 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. During the period under notice the Inspector has reported to me on many occasions that he has had great difficulty in obtaining several samples in one locality, in fact in several instances he has found that after taking one sample in a neighbourhood before he can get to the next shop information has been given to other traders in the neighbourhood. On one occasion, a Saturday night, two loafers saw the officer sampling in a shop and made it their business to call at every shop in that road, doubtless to give information to the traders. This is difficult of proof, and even if proved could not in law be held to be obstruction, but from the reports received from other districts there is no doubt that such men are recognised and find it to their advantage. To those intimately acquainted with the work of detecting adulteration it is well known that some fraudulent traders make it a practice to sell adulterated goods intermittently, so as to have a better chance of evading detection, and it does not necessarily follow that because an adulterated sample is sold in one instance the article supplied to another purchaser will also be adulterated. In one case reported to the Board of Agriculture, suspicion as to the methods of a certain trader having been aroused, no less than 40 test samples were taken and analysed, 28 of these were reported genuine whilst 12 were illegal mixtures. During the year considerable attention has been directed to procuring samples on Friday and Saturday nights, and also to sampling milk in the early morning, as it was felt that it was principally during the busy marketing hours that fraud was practised, and the results obtained have amply justified that conclusion. In only three cases during the year have summonses been dismissed owing to the defendants pleading the warranty clause. In each instance the article in question was milk. As I pointed out in my last Report every effort should be made by Local Authorities to procure the abolition of this, the easiest defence that can possibly be set up, as it is a constant menace to the effective prevention of adulteration. It is my duty to again call attention to the great increase in the number of itinerant milk vendors who are selling separated 207 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. milk in the Borough. The evils attached to the sale of this commodity are much greater than would at first appear. If separated milk is sold as such well and good, but I know from experience that much of the evil practices in connection with the milk trade is due solely to this source. The following are a few instances which have been brought under my notice during the year :— A vendor residing outside this Borough—as indeed most of them do—advertised very extensively by means of handbills that he was prepared to deliver pure new milk three times daily at 2jd. per quart. The Inspector endeavoured to procure a sample, but he found on applying for it the seller stated that he had three kinds of milk, viz.:—new milk, hand-skimmed milk, and machine-skimmed or separated milk, and he could have which he wished. Knowing that there was no hand-skimmed milk consigned to the Borough the Inspector then asked him where he procured the article, and he obtained the somewhat astonishing answer that "he made it." Upon inquiring further how, the officer was informed that half a churn of new milk was mixed with half a churn of separated milk, and that hand-skimmed milk was the result. This article is undoubtedly foisted on the unsuspicious poor for genuine new milk. It is also interesting to note that in every instance the vessels containing these various milks are distinctly marked, but needless to say it is impossible for the ordinary purchaser to notice this, as the inscription being on the top of the lid when the can is closed, it follows that it cannot be seen when it is open and the vendor is serving. Another instance of the same kind :— A., an itinerant seller, procured a small contract to supply a cookery school in the Borough at a uniform price of 4d. per quart, the ordinary retail price. Information reached the Inspector as to this, and knowing the character of the dealer in question, he managed, after some considerable difficulty, to obtain a sample, which proved on analysis to be deficient in cream to the extent of 208 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 26 per cent. In this case the magistrate inflicted a penalty of £20 with £3 5s. costs. The sale of separated milk offers very great temptation to the ordinary milk carrier, that is, the servant employed by the retailer, and it is known that many of these separated milk vendors sell a quantity to carriers in order that the said carriers may supplement their wages. This is a grave menace to the honest retailer, who is entirely at the mercy of his servant, for as the present law stands the master is liable for the action of his servant, and in one instance during the year the Inspector was successful in actually seeing the adulteration committed. In any future legislation with regard to milk it is imperative that the sale of separated milk should be prohibited entirely, or as an alternative it should only be sold from churns and vessels distinctly coloured, say a bright red, as is done in Chicago, and that new milk shall not be sold from the same barrow or cart. The public would thus be protected from the substitution of the inferior for the genuine article. Whilst dealing with this subject I may point out the diminution in the number of cases in which milk is found to be adulterated with water. This class of offence is rapidly dying out, and in my opinion it is principally due to the facilities which obtain at the present time for the dishonest trader to obtain separated milk, there being not so great a risk of detection in the addition of separated milk to new milk as in the old method of dilution by water. I am pleased to report that in no instance during the year has it been necessary to institute proceedings against any tradesman for refusing to serve the Inspector, or for obstructing him in the execution of his duty. 209 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Staff of the Department. An additional Sanitary Inspector was appointed towards the -close of the year, and the following report was made as to the alterations of the districts that would be necessary. The report was adopted by the Council, and the new Inspector, who was transferred from the Borough Engineer's Department, commenced his duties on the 27th November, 1908. REPORT OF THE MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH ON THE ALTERATION OF SANITARY INSPECTORS' DISTRICTS. No. of District. Name of Sanitary Inspector. Old District. Estimated Population, 1907. Area. Population, 1901. 1 G. Russll 2,218 25,842 30,210 2 S. Crane 1.264 23.405 38.145 3 C. Hatton 450 25.000 25.000 4 A. J. Weeks 810 26,000 34.935 5 W. G. Crabbe 53' 34.516 44.260 6 H. E. Brown S29 25,864 38.030 7 S. G. Fairchild 557 32.726 34,500 8 B. Nurcotnbe 838 31.694 38,720 9 E. B. Kershaw 1,631 28 227 44,000 210 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. PROPOSED NEW DISTRICTS. No. of District. Name of Sanitary Inspector. Area. Estimated Population, 1908. 1 G. Russell 2,218 30,900 3 S. Crane 1,200 35.950 3 C. Hatton 5J4 28,200 4 A. J. Weeks 810 35.950 5 W. G. Crabbe 531 44,400 6 R. Brown 682 27,500 7 S. G. Fairchild 487 32,000 8 B. Nurcombe 588 36,200 9 H. E. Brown 1,528 37.000 10 E. B. Kershaw 570 27.450 "No. 1 district, Putney Ward. No change is necessary in this district, the present Inspector being able to overtake the work. "No. 2 district. Owing to the large increase in the population of the Southfield Ward, and owing to the large area of the district, I would propose that a portion of this district be taken away and added to No. 3 district, reducing the area to 1,200 acres, and the estimated population to 35,950. "No. 3 district has not appreciably increased in population since the Census in 1901, and I would propose that a portion of No. 2 district be added to this. This would increase the area to 514 acres and the population to 28,200. "No. 4 district. No change. "No. 5 district. No change. 211 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "No. 6 district. With regard to No. 6 district, which included the whole of Tooting and the southern portion of Balham Wards, I would propose that a considerable portion of Tooting be added to No. 10 district. No. 6 district would then have an area of 682 acres and a population of 27,500. During the past seven years the number of cases of infectious diseases has increased considerably, as well as the amount of sanitary supervision necessary. I would also propose that the new Inspector, Mr. Robert Brown, be appointed to this district. "No. 7 district. The old district included a portion of the South Ward, Clapham, but owing to the large increase in the population of the South Ward and the proposed division of the Streatham Ward I would propose that the North Ward, Clapham, should constitute one Sanitary Inspector's district, with an area of 487 acres and an estimated population of 32,000. "No. 8 district. With regard to No. 8 district, which included a portion of Streatham, I would propose that this district should comprise the whole of the South Ward, Clapham, which has an area of 588 acres and an estimated population of 36,200. "No. 9 district. This district would include the greater portion of Streatham Ward with an area of 1,528 acres and an estimated population of 37,000. This district, although large in area and in population, has a considerable amount of land which is not at present built on, and it also contains the whole of Streatham Common and a portion of Tooting Bec Common. The houses are inhabited by persons of good social standing, and a large proportion of the district does not at present require much sanitary supervision. I would propose that Mr. Herbert E. Brown, who is at present the Inspector for No. 6 district, should be appointed to this district, and that he should reside in the district. 212 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "'No. 10 district. This district would include the rest of Streatham and a portion of Tooting. Although the area is only 570 acres and the estimated population 27,450, yet in all probability in a few years there will be a considerable increase in population. That part of the district which is situated in Tooting Ward requires a large amount of sanitary supervision." I have again much pleasure in reporting that the whole of the work of the Department was carried out by the Inspectors and the Clerical Staff to my entire satisfaction. I have the honour to be, Mr. Mayor and Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, P. CALDWELL SMITH, Medical Officer of Health. APPENDIX. During the year 1908 the Inspector brought to the South London Central Public Laboratory 1,004 samples of Food and Drugs, classifiable as under:— Dairy Produce Groceries Drugs Meats Flour and Cereals Confectionery and Jam 882 samples. 63 ,, 22 „ 20 „ 14 ,, 3 .» 1,004 samples.
